<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Disease resulting in compulsive engagement in rewarding stimuli despite adverse consequences</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Addictive" redirects here. <p class="mw-empty-elt">

<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Medical condition</div><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="infobox" style="width:402px; ; border-collapse:collapse;"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="font-size:100%; background:#FFCC66; box-shadow: inset 3px 3px 0 #fafafa, inset -3px -3px 0 #fafafa;; padding:5px;">Addiction and dependence glossary<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cellular_basis-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Addiction_glossary_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Addiction_glossary-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_Labs_Glossary_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler_Labs_Glossary-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brain_disease_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brain_disease-2">[2]</a></sup></th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2" style="text-align:left; padding-left:0;">
<ul><li><b><a class="mw-selflink selflink">addiction</a></b> – a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biopsychosocial" title="Biopsychosocial">biopsychosocial</a> disorder characterized by persistent use of drugs (including alcohol) despite substantial harm and adverse consequences</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-selflink selflink">addictive drug</a></b> – psychoactive substances that with repeated use are associated with significantly higher rates of substance use disorders, due in large part to the drug's effect on brain <a href="/wiki/Reward_system" title="Reward system">reward systems</a></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Substance_dependence" title="Substance dependence">dependence</a></b> – an adaptive state associated with a withdrawal syndrome upon cessation of repeated exposure to a stimulus (e.g., drug intake)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Reverse_tolerance" title="Reverse tolerance">drug sensitization</a></b> or <b>reverse tolerance</b> – the escalating effect of a drug resulting from repeated administration at a given dose</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Drug_withdrawal" title="Drug withdrawal">drug withdrawal</a></b> – symptoms that occur upon cessation of repeated drug use</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Physical_dependence" title="Physical dependence">physical dependence</a></b> – dependence that involves persistent physical–<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/somatic" title="wikt:somatic">somatic</a> withdrawal symptoms (e.g., fatigue and <a href="/wiki/Delirium_tremens" title="Delirium tremens">delirium tremens</a>)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Psychological_dependence" title="Psychological dependence">psychological dependence</a></b> – dependence that involves emotional–motivational withdrawal symptoms (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Dysphoria" title="Dysphoria">dysphoria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anhedonia" title="Anhedonia">anhedonia</a>)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Reinforcement" title="Reinforcement">reinforcing</a> stimuli</b> – stimuli that increase the probability of repeating behaviors paired with them</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Reward_system" title="Reward system">rewarding</a> stimuli</b> – stimuli that the brain interprets as intrinsically positive and desirable or as something to approach</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Sensitization" title="Sensitization">sensitization</a></b> – an amplified response to a stimulus resulting from repeated exposure to it</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Substance_use_disorder" title="Substance use disorder">substance use disorder</a></b> – a condition in which the use of substances leads to clinically and functionally significant impairment or distress</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Drug_tolerance" title="Drug tolerance">tolerance</a></b> – the diminishing effect of a drug resulting from repeated administration at a given dose</li></ul>
</td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-below" colspan="2" style="text-align:right; padding-top:0; padding-right:5px;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Addiction_glossary" title="Template:Addiction glossary"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Addiction_glossary" title="Template talk:Addiction glossary"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Addiction_glossary&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Addiction</b> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neuropsychological" title="Neuropsychological">neuropsychological</a> disorder characterized by a persistent and intense urge to engage in certain behaviors, often usage of a <a href="/wiki/Drug" title="Drug">drug</a>,  despite substantial harm and other negative consequences. Repetitive drug use often alters <a href="/wiki/Brain#Function" title="Brain">brain function</a> in ways that perpetuate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Craving_(withdrawal)" title="Craving (withdrawal)">craving</a>, and weakens (but does not completely negate) <a href="/wiki/Self-control" title="Self-control">self-control</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heilig-et-al-2021_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heilig-et-al-2021-6">[6]</a></sup> This phenomenon – drugs <a href="/wiki/Neuroplasticity" title="Neuroplasticity">reshaping</a> <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">brain</a> function – has led to an understanding of addiction as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brain_disorder" title="Brain disorder">brain disorder</a> with a complex variety of <a href="/wiki/Social_psychology" title="Social psychology">psychosocial</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience" title="Neuroscience">neurobiological</a> (and thus involuntary)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[a]</a></sup> factors that are implicated in addiction's development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cellular_basis-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIDA2020_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIDA2020-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> Classic signs of addiction include <a href="/wiki/Compulsive_behavior" title="Compulsive behavior">compulsive</a> engagement in <a href="/wiki/Reward_system" title="Reward system">rewarding</a> stimuli, <i>preoccupation</i> with substances or behavior, and continued use despite negative consequences. <a href="/wiki/Habit" title="Habit">Habits</a> and patterns associated with addiction are typically characterized by immediate <a href="/wiki/Gratification" title="Gratification">gratification</a> (short-term reward),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18790142_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18790142-10">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHM_addiction-reward-reinforcement_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-NHM_addiction-reward-reinforcement-11">[10]</a></sup> coupled with delayed deleterious effects (long-term costs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIDA2020_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-NIDA2020-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid3278676_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid3278676-12">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Examples of drug and behavioral addictions include <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism" title="Alcoholism">alcoholism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cannabis_use_disorder" title="Cannabis use disorder">marijuana addiction</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphetamine_addiction" title="Amphetamine addiction">amphetamine addiction</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cocaine_addiction" title="Cocaine addiction">cocaine addiction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicotine#Reinforcement_disorders" title="Nicotine">nicotine addiction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Opioid_use_disorder" title="Opioid use disorder">opioid addiction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Video_game_addiction" title="Video game addiction">video game addiction</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gambling_addiction" title="Gambling addiction">gambling addiction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pornography_addiction" title="Pornography addiction">pornography addiction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sexual_addiction" title="Sexual addiction">sexual addiction</a>. The only behavioral addiction recognized by the <a href="/wiki/DSM-5" title="DSM-5">DSM-5</a> and the <a href="/wiki/ICD-10" title="ICD-10">ICD-10</a> is gambling addiction. With the introduction of the ICD-11 gaming addiction was appended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> The term "addiction" is frequently misused when referring to other compulsive behaviors or disorders, particularly <a href="/wiki/Substance_dependence" title="Substance dependence">dependence</a>, in news media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Addiction-dependence_distinction_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Addiction-dependence_distinction-14">[13]</a></sup> An important distinction between drug addiction and dependence is that drug dependence is a disorder in which cessation of drug use results in an unpleasant state of <a href="/wiki/Drug_withdrawal" title="Drug withdrawal">withdrawal</a>, which can lead to further drug use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHMH_3e_terms-DSM_flaw_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-NHMH_3e_terms-DSM_flaw-15">[14]</a></sup> Addiction is the compulsive use of a substance or performance of a behavior that is independent of withdrawal. Addiction can occur in the absence of dependence, and dependence can occur in the absence of addiction, although the two often occur together.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavioral_addiction">Behavioral addiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Behavioral addiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Behavioral_addiction" title="Behavioral addiction">Behavioral addiction</a></div>
<p>The term "behavioral addiction" refers to a <a href="/wiki/Compulsive_behavior" title="Compulsive behavior">compulsion</a> to engage in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_reward" title="Natural reward">natural reward</a> – which is a behavior that is inherently rewarding (i.e., desirable or appealing) – despite adverse consequences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHM_addiction-reward-reinforcement_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-NHM_addiction-reward-reinforcement-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler-17">[16]</a></sup> Preclinical evidence has demonstrated that marked increases in the expression of ΔFosB through repetitive and excessive exposure to a natural reward induces the same behavioral effects and <a href="/wiki/Neuroplasticity" title="Neuroplasticity">neuroplasticity</a> as occurs in a drug addiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Systematic_review_-_yet_another_DSM_fail_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Systematic_review_-_yet_another_DSM_fail-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_D1_signaling_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_D1_signaling-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_NMDA_signaling_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_NMDA_signaling-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Reviews of both clinical research in humans and preclinical studies involving ΔFosB have identified compulsive sexual activity – specifically, any form of <a href="/wiki/Sexual_intercourse" title="Sexual intercourse">sexual intercourse</a> – as an addiction (i.e., <a href="/wiki/Sexual_addiction" title="Sexual addiction">sexual addiction</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Systematic_review_-_yet_another_DSM_fail_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Systematic_review_-_yet_another_DSM_fail-18">[17]</a></sup> Moreover, <a href="#Reward_sensitization">reward cross-sensitization</a> between <a href="/wiki/Amphetamine" title="Amphetamine">amphetamine</a> and sexual activity, meaning that exposure to one increases the desire for both, has been shown to occur preclinically and clinically as a <a href="/wiki/Dopamine_dysregulation_syndrome" title="Dopamine dysregulation syndrome">dopamine dysregulation syndrome</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Systematic_review_-_yet_another_DSM_fail_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Systematic_review_-_yet_another_DSM_fail-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_D1_signaling_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_D1_signaling-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_NMDA_signaling_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_NMDA_signaling-20">[19]</a></sup> ΔFosB <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">expression</a> is required for this cross-sensitization effect, which intensifies with the level of ΔFosB expression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_D1_signaling_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_D1_signaling-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_NMDA_signaling_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_NMDA_signaling-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Reviews of preclinical studies indicate that long-term frequent and excessive consumption of high fat or sugar foods can produce an addiction (<a href="/wiki/Food_addiction" title="Food addiction">food addiction</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-5"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler-17">[16]</a></sup> This can include <a href="/wiki/Chocolate" title="Chocolate">chocolate</a>. Chocolates' sweet flavour and pharmacological ingredients is known to create a strong craving or feel 'addictive' by the consumer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> A person who has a strong liking for chocolate may refer to themselves as a <a href="/wiki/Chocoholic" title="Chocoholic">chocoholic</a>. Chocolate is not yet formally recognised by the DSM-5 as a diagnosable addiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gambling provides a natural reward which is associated with compulsive behavior and for which clinical diagnostic manuals, namely the <a href="/wiki/DSM-5" title="DSM-5">DSM-5</a>, have identified diagnostic criteria for an "addiction".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-6"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup> In order for a person's gambling behavior to meet criteria of an addiction, it shows certain characteristics, such as mood modification, compulsivity, and withdrawal. There is evidence from functional neuroimaging that gambling activates the reward system and the <a href="/wiki/Mesolimbic_pathway" title="Mesolimbic pathway">mesolimbic pathway</a> in particular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-7"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Behavioral_addictions_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Behavioral_addictions-23">[22]</a></sup> Similarly, shopping and playing video games are associated with compulsive behaviors in humans and have also been shown to activate the mesolimbic pathway and other parts of the reward system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-8"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup> Based upon this evidence, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gambling_addiction" title="Gambling addiction">gambling addiction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Video_game_addiction" title="Video game addiction">video game addiction</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shopping_addiction" title="Shopping addiction">shopping addiction</a> are classified accordingly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-9"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Behavioral_addictions_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Behavioral_addictions-23">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_and_symptoms">Signs and symptoms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Signs and symptoms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">July 2022</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personality_theories">Personality theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Personality theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Personality_theories_of_addiction" title="Personality theories of addiction">Personality theories of addiction</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Personality_theories_of_addiction" title="Personality theories of addiction">Personality theories of addiction</a> are <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychological</a> models that associate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Personality_traits" title="Personality traits">personality traits</a> or modes of thinking (i.e., <a href="/wiki/Affective_science" title="Affective science">affective states</a>) with an individual's proclivity for developing an addiction. <a href="/wiki/Data_analysis" title="Data analysis">Data analysis</a> demonstrates that there is a significant difference in the psychological profiles of drug users and non-users and the psychological predisposition to using different drugs may be different.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> Models of addiction risk that have been proposed in psychology literature include an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Affect_dysregulation" title="Affect dysregulation">affect dysregulation</a> model of positive and negative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychological_affect" title="Psychological affect">psychological affects</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Reinforcement_sensitivity_theory" title="Reinforcement sensitivity theory">reinforcement sensitivity theory</a> model of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Impulsiveness" title="Impulsiveness">impulsiveness</a> and behavioral inhibition, and an impulsivity model of <a href="#Sensitization">reward sensitization</a> and impulsiveness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cheetham_2010_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cheetham_2010-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Franken_2006_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Franken_2006-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18232522_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18232522-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kimbrel_2007_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kimbrel_2007-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawe_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dawe-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neuropsychology">Neuropsychology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Neuropsychology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cognitive_control" title="Cognitive control">Cognitive control</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stimulus_control" title="Stimulus control">stimulus control</a>, which is associated with <a href="/wiki/Operant_conditioning" title="Operant conditioning">operant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Classical_conditioning" title="Classical conditioning">classical conditioning</a>, represent opposite processes (i.e., internal vs external or environmental, respectively) that compete over the control of an individual's elicited behaviors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cognitive_-_stimulus_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cognitive_-_stimulus-30">[29]</a></sup> Cognitive control, and particularly <a href="/wiki/Inhibitory_control" title="Inhibitory control">inhibitory control over behavior</a>, is impaired in both addiction and <a href="/wiki/Attention_deficit_hyperactivity_disorder" title="Attention deficit hyperactivity disorder">attention deficit hyperactivity disorder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Executive_functions_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Executive_functions-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHM-Cognitive_Control_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-NHM-Cognitive_Control-32">[31]</a></sup> Stimulus-driven behavioral responses (i.e., stimulus control) that are associated with a particular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rewarding_stimulus" title="Rewarding stimulus">rewarding stimulus</a> tend to dominate one's behavior in an addiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHM-Cognitive_Control_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-NHM-Cognitive_Control-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stimulus_control_of_behavior">Stimulus control of behavior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Stimulus control of behavior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Stimulus_control" title="Stimulus control">Stimulus control</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cognitive_control_of_behavior">Cognitive control of behavior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Cognitive control of behavior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cognitive_control" title="Cognitive control">Cognitive control</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Risk_factors">Risk factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Risk factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Addiction_vulnerability" title="Addiction vulnerability">Addiction vulnerability</a></div>
<p>There are a number of genetic and environmental risk factors for developing an addiction, that vary across the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cellular_basis-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction-33">[32]</a></sup> Genetic and environmental risk factors each account for roughly half of an individual's risk for developing an addiction;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-Cellular_basis-3">[3]</a></sup> the contribution from epigenetic risk factors to the total risk is unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction-33">[32]</a></sup> Even in individuals with a relatively low genetic risk, exposure to sufficiently high doses of an addictive drug for a long period of time (e.g., weeks–months) can result in an addiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-Cellular_basis-3">[3]</a></sup> Adverse childhood events are associated with negative health outcomes, such as substance use disorder. Studies show that childhood abuse or exposure to violent crime was positively related to developing a mood or anxiety disorder, as well as a substance dependence risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_factors">Genetic factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Genetic factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism#Genetic_variation" title="Alcoholism">Alcoholism § Genetic variation</a></div>
<p>Genetic factors, along with environmental (e.g., psychosocial) factors, have been established as significant contributors to addiction vulnerability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-Cellular_basis-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction-33">[32]</a></sup> Studies done on 350 hospitalized drug-dependent patients showed that over half met the criteria for alcohol abuse, with a role of familial factors being prevalent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Epidemiological_method" title="Epidemiological method">Epidemiological</a> studies estimate that genetic factors account for 40–60% of the risk factors for <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism" title="Alcoholism">alcoholism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> Similar rates of heritability for other types of drug addiction have been indicated by other studies, specifically in genes that encode the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha5_Nicotinic_Acetylcholine_Receptor" title="Alpha5 Nicotinic Acetylcholine Receptor">Alpha5 Nicotinic Acetylcholine Receptor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kendler94_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kendler94-37">[36]</a></sup> Knestler hypothesized in 1964 that a gene or group of genes might contribute to predisposition to addiction in several ways. For example, altered levels of a normal protein due to environmental factors may change the structure or functioning of specific brain neurons during development. These altered brain neurons could affect the susceptibility of an individual to an initial drug use experience. In support of this hypothesis, animal studies have shown that environmental factors such as stress can affect an animal's genetic expression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kendler94_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kendler94-37">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>In humans, twin studies into addiction have provided some of the highest-quality evidence of this link, with results finding that if one twin is affected by addiction, the other twin is likely to be as well, and often to the same substance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crowe1991_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crowe1991-38">[37]</a></sup> Further evidence of a genetic component is research findings from family studies which suggest that if one family member has a history of addiction, the chances of a relative or close family developing those same habits are much higher than one who has not been introduced to addiction at a young age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, the data implicating specific genes in the development of drug addiction is mixed for most genes. Many addiction studies that aim to identify specific genes focus on common variants with an allele frequency of greater than 5% in the general population; however, when associated with disease, these only confer a small amount of additional risk with an odds ratio of 1.1–1.3 percent; this has led to the development the rare variant hypothesis, which states that genes with low frequencies in the population (&lt;1%) confer much greater additional risk in the development of the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23454283_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid23454283-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Genome-wide_association_study" title="Genome-wide association study">Genome-wide association studies</a> (GWAS) are used to examine genetic associations with dependence, addiction, and drug use; however, these studies rarely identify genes from proteins previously described via animal knockout models and candidate gene analysis. Instead, large percentages of genes involved in processes such as cell adhesion are commonly identified. The important effects of <a href="/wiki/Endophenotype" title="Endophenotype">endophenotypes</a> are typically not capable of being captured by these methods. Genes identified in GWAS for drug addiction may be involved either in adjusting brain behavior prior to drug experiences, subsequent to them, or both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_factors">Environmental factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Environmental factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Environmental risk factors for addiction are the experiences of an individual during their lifetime that interact with the individual's genetic composition to increase or decrease his or her vulnerability to addiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-Cellular_basis-3">[3]</a></sup> For example, after the nationwide outbreak of COVID-19, more people quit (vs. started) smoking; and smokers, on average, reduced the quantity of cigarettes they consumed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> More generally, a number of different environmental factors have been implicated as risk factors for addiction, including various psychosocial stressors. The <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_on_Drug_Abuse" title="National Institute on Drug Abuse">National Institute on Drug Abuse</a> (NIDA) cites lack of parental supervision, the prevalence of peer substance use, drug availability, and poverty as risk factors for substance use among children and adolescents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abuse_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abuse-43">[42]</a></sup> The brain disease model of addiction posits that an individual's exposure to an addictive drug is the most significant environmental risk factor for addiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> However, many researchers, including neuroscientists, indicate that the brain disease model presents a misleading, incomplete, and potentially detrimental explanation of addiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Adverse childhood experiences (ACEs) are various forms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Child_maltreatment" title="Child maltreatment">maltreatment</a> and household dysfunction experienced in childhood. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adverse_Childhood_Experiences_Study" title="Adverse Childhood Experiences Study">Adverse Childhood Experiences Study</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a> has shown a strong <a href="/wiki/Dose%E2%80%93response_relationship" title="Dose–response relationship">dose–response relationship</a> between ACEs and numerous health, social, and behavioral problems throughout a person's lifespan, including <a href="/wiki/Substance_use_disorder" title="Substance use disorder">substance use disorder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SAMHSA_ACE_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-SAMHSA_ACE-46">[45]</a></sup> Children's neurological development can be permanently disrupted when they are chronically exposed to stressful events such as physical, emotional, or sexual abuse, physical or emotional neglect, witnessing violence in the household, or a parent being incarcerated or having a mental illness. As a result, the child's cognitive functioning or ability to cope with negative or disruptive emotions may be impaired. Over time, the child may adopt substance use as a coping mechanism, particularly during <a href="/wiki/Adolescence" title="Adolescence">adolescence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SAMHSA_ACE_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-SAMHSA_ACE-46">[45]</a></sup> A study of 900 court cases involving children who experienced abuse found that a vast amount of them went on to have some form of addiction in their adolescence or adult life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enoch2011_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Enoch2011-47">[46]</a></sup> This pathway towards addiction that is opened through stressful experiences during childhood can be avoided by a change in environmental factors throughout an individual's life and opportunities of professional help.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enoch2011_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Enoch2011-47">[46]</a></sup> If one has friends or peers who engage in drug use favorably, the chances of them developing an addiction increases. Family conflict and home management is also a cause for one to become engaged in alcohol or other drug use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Age">Age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Adolescence represents a period of unique vulnerability for developing an addiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> In adolescence, the incentive-rewards systems in the brain mature well before the cognitive control center. This consequentially grants the incentive-rewards systems a disproportionate amount of power in the behavioral decision-making process. Therefore, adolescents are increasingly likely to act on their impulses and engage in risky, potentially addicting behavior before considering the consequences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> Not only are adolescents more likely to initiate and maintain drug use, but once addicted they are more resistant to treatment and more liable to relapse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most individuals are exposed to and use addictive drugs for the first time during their teenage years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drug_use_trends_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Drug_use_trends-53">[52]</a></sup> In the United States, there were just over 2.8 million new users of illicit drugs in 2013 (~7,800 new users per day);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drug_use_trends_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Drug_use_trends-53">[52]</a></sup> among them, 54.1% were under 18 years of age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drug_use_trends_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-Drug_use_trends-53">[52]</a></sup> In 2011, there were approximately 20.6 million people in the United States over the age of 12 with an addiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-54">[53]</a></sup> Over 90% of those with an addiction began drinking, smoking or using illicit drugs before the age of 18.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-54">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Comorbid_disorders">Comorbid disorders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Comorbid disorders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Individuals with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comorbid" title="Comorbid">comorbid</a> (i.e., co-occurring) <a href="/wiki/Mental_health" title="Mental health">mental health</a> disorders such as depression, anxiety, attention-deficit/hyperactivity disorder (ADHD) or post-traumatic stress disorder are more likely to develop substance use disorders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_on_Drug_Abuse" title="National Institute on Drug Abuse"><abbr title="National Institute on Drug Abuse">NIDA</abbr></a> cites early aggressive behavior as a risk factor for substance use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abuse_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Abuse-43">[42]</a></sup> A study by the <a href="/wiki/National_Bureau_of_Economic_Research" title="National Bureau of Economic Research">National Bureau of Economic Research</a> found that there is a "definite connection between mental illness and the use of addictive substances" and a majority of mental health patients participate in the use of these substances: 38% alcohol, 44% cocaine, and 40% cigarettes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Epigenetic">Epigenetic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Epigenetic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance">Transgenerational epigenetic inheritance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Transgenerational epigenetic inheritance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance" title="Transgenerational epigenetic inheritance">Transgenerational epigenetic inheritance</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epigenetic" title="Epigenetic">Epigenetic</a> genes and their products (e.g., proteins) are the key components through which environmental influences can affect the genes of an individual;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction-33">[32]</a></sup> they also serve as the mechanism responsible for <a href="/wiki/Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance" title="Transgenerational epigenetic inheritance">transgenerational epigenetic inheritance</a>, a phenomenon in which environmental influences on the genes of a parent can affect the associated traits and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Behavioral_phenotype" title="Behavioral phenotype">behavioral phenotypes</a> of their offspring (e.g., behavioral responses to environmental stimuli).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction_33-4"><a href="#cite_note-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction-33">[32]</a></sup> In addiction, epigenetic mechanisms play a central role in the <a href="/wiki/Pathophysiology" title="Pathophysiology">pathophysiology</a> of the disease;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-Cellular_basis-3">[3]</a></sup> it has been noted that some of the alterations to the <a href="/wiki/Epigenome" title="Epigenome">epigenome</a> which arise through chronic exposure to addictive stimuli during an addiction can be transmitted across generations, in turn affecting the behavior of one's children (e.g., the child's behavioral responses to addictive drugs and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_reward" title="Natural reward">natural rewards</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction_33-5"><a href="#cite_note-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26572641_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26572641-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>The general classes of epigenetic alterations that have been implicated in transgenerational epigenetic inheritance include <a href="/wiki/DNA_methylation" title="DNA methylation">DNA methylation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Histone_modification" title="Histone modification">histone modifications</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Downregulation_and_upregulation" title="Downregulation and upregulation">downregulation or upregulation</a> of <a href="/wiki/MicroRNA" title="MicroRNA">microRNAs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction_33-6"><a href="#cite_note-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction-33">[32]</a></sup> With respect to addiction, more research is needed to determine the specific <a href="/wiki/Heredity" title="Heredity">heritable</a> epigenetic alterations that arise from various forms of addiction in humans and the corresponding behavioral phenotypes from these epigenetic alterations that occur in human offspring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction_33-7"><a href="#cite_note-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26572641_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26572641-59">[58]</a></sup> Based upon preclinical evidence from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_research" title="Animal research">animal research</a>, certain addiction-induced epigenetic alterations in rats can be transmitted from parent to offspring and produce behavioral phenotypes that decrease the offspring's risk of developing an addiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[note 1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction_33-13"><a href="#cite_note-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction-33">[32]</a></sup> More generally, the heritable behavioral phenotypes that are derived from addiction-induced epigenetic alterations and transmitted from parent to offspring may serve to either increase or decrease the offspring's risk of developing an addiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction_33-14"><a href="#cite_note-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26572641_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26572641-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanisms">Mechanisms<span class="anchor" id="Biomolecular_mechanisms"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Mechanisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Addiction is a disorder of the brain's reward system developing through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcriptional" title="Transcriptional">transcriptional</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epigenetic" title="Epigenetic">epigenetic</a> mechanisms as a result of chronically high levels of exposure to an addictive stimulus (e.g., eating food, the use of cocaine, engagement in sexual activity, participation in high-thrill cultural activities such as gambling, etc.) over extended time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-8"><a href="#cite_note-Cellular_basis-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-What_the_ΔFosB?_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-What_the_ΔFosB?-61">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-10"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/FOSB#DeltaFosB" title="FOSB">DeltaFosB</a> (ΔFosB), a gene <a href="/wiki/Transcription_factor" title="Transcription factor">transcription factor</a>, is a critical component and common factor in the development of virtually all forms of behavioral and drug addictions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-What_the_ΔFosB?_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-What_the_ΔFosB?-61">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-11"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G9a_reverses_ΔFosB_plasticity_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-G9a_reverses_ΔFosB_plasticity-62">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler-17">[16]</a></sup> Two decades of research into ΔFosB's role in addiction have demonstrated that addiction arises, and the associated compulsive behavior intensifies or attenuates, along with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overexpression" title="Overexpression">overexpression</a> of ΔFosB in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D1-type" title="D1-type">D1-type</a> <a href="/wiki/Medium_spiny_neuron" title="Medium spiny neuron">medium spiny neurons</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Nucleus_accumbens" title="Nucleus accumbens">nucleus accumbens</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-9"><a href="#cite_note-Cellular_basis-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-What_the_ΔFosB?_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-What_the_ΔFosB?-61">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-12"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G9a_reverses_ΔFosB_plasticity_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-G9a_reverses_ΔFosB_plasticity-62">[60]</a></sup> Due to the causal relationship between ΔFosB expression and addictions, it is used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Preclinical_research" title="Preclinical research">preclinically</a> as an addiction <a href="/wiki/Biomarker_(medicine)" title="Biomarker (medicine)">biomarker</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-10"><a href="#cite_note-Cellular_basis-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-What_the_ΔFosB?_61-3"><a href="#cite_note-What_the_ΔFosB?-61">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G9a_reverses_ΔFosB_plasticity_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-G9a_reverses_ΔFosB_plasticity-62">[60]</a></sup> ΔFosB expression in these neurons directly and positively regulates drug <a href="/wiki/Self-administration" title="Self-administration">self-administration</a> and <a href="#Sensitization">reward sensitization</a> through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Positive_reinforcement" title="Positive reinforcement">positive reinforcement</a>, while decreasing sensitivity to aversion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ΔFosB_behaviors_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-ΔFosB_behaviors-63">[note 2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-11"><a href="#cite_note-Cellular_basis-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-What_the_ΔFosB?_61-4"><a href="#cite_note-What_the_ΔFosB?-61">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="infobox" style="width:610px; ; border-collapse:collapse;"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="font-size:100%; background:#FFCC66; box-shadow: inset 3px 3px 0 #fafafa, inset -3px -3px 0 #fafafa;; padding:5px;">Transcription factor glossary</th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2" style="text-align:left; padding-left:0;">
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">gene expression</a></b> – the process by which information from a <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">gene</a> is used in the synthesis of a functional gene product such as a <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a></li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcription_(genetics)" title="Transcription (genetics)">transcription</a></b> – the process of making <a href="/wiki/Messenger_RNA" title="Messenger RNA">messenger RNA</a> (mRNA) from a <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> template by <a href="/wiki/RNA_polymerase" title="RNA polymerase">RNA polymerase</a></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Transcription_factor" title="Transcription factor">transcription factor</a></b> – a protein that binds to DNA and regulates gene expression by promoting or suppressing transcription</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Transcriptional_regulation" title="Transcriptional regulation">transcriptional regulation</a></b> – <i>controlling</i> the rate of gene transcription for example by helping or hindering RNA polymerase binding to DNA</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upregulation" title="Upregulation">upregulation</a></b>, <b>activation</b>, or <b>promotion</b> – <i>increase</i> the rate of gene transcription</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Downregulation" title="Downregulation">downregulation</a></b>, <b>repression</b>, or <b>suppression</b> – <i>decrease</i> the rate of gene transcription</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Coactivator_(genetics)" title="Coactivator (genetics)">coactivator</a></b> – a protein (or a small molecule) that works with transcription factors to <i>increase</i> the rate of gene transcription</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corepressor_(genetics)" title="Corepressor (genetics)">corepressor</a></b> – a protein (or a small molecule) that works with transcription factors to <i>decrease</i> the rate of gene transcription</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Response_element" title="Response element">response element</a></b> – a specific sequence of DNA that a transcription factor binds to</li></ul>
</td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-below" colspan="2" style="text-align:right; padding-top:0; padding-right:5px;"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Transcription_factor_glossary" title="Template:Transcription factor glossary"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Transcription_factor_glossary" title="Template talk:Transcription factor glossary"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Transcription_factor_glossary&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>

<div id="annotation_495x35" style="position:absolute; left:495px; top:35px; line-height:110%;"><span style="background-color:transparent;"><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[Color legend 1]</a></sup></span></div></div>
