<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><div style="float:left;margin-right:0.5em"><img alt="The image above contains clickable links" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="133" decoding="async" height="27" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Interactive_icon.svg/18px-Interactive_icon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Interactive_icon.svg/27px-Interactive_icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Interactive_icon.svg/36px-Interactive_icon.svg.png 2x" title="The image above contains clickable links" width="18"/></div>This diagram depicts the signaling events in the <a href="/wiki/Mesolimbic_pathway" title="Mesolimbic pathway">brain's reward center</a> that are induced by chronic high-dose exposure to psychostimulants that increase the concentration of synaptic dopamine, like <a href="/wiki/Amphetamine" title="Amphetamine">amphetamine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methamphetamine" title="Methamphetamine">methamphetamine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Phenethylamine" title="Phenethylamine">phenethylamine</a>. Following presynaptic <a href="/wiki/Dopamine" title="Dopamine">dopamine</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glutamate" title="Glutamate">glutamate</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cotransmission" title="Cotransmission">co-release</a> by such psychostimulants,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler-Renthal_Figure_2_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler-Renthal_Figure_2-65">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glutamate-dopamine_cotransmission_review_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glutamate-dopamine_cotransmission_review-66">[62]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter_receptor" title="Neurotransmitter receptor">postsynaptic receptors</a> for these <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitters</a> trigger internal signaling events through a <a href="/wiki/CAMP-dependent_pathway" title="CAMP-dependent pathway">cAMP-dependent pathway</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Calcium_signaling" title="Calcium signaling">calcium-dependent pathway</a> that ultimately result in increased <abbr title="cAMP response element-binding protein">CREB</abbr> phosphorylation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler-Renthal_Figure_2_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler-Renthal_Figure_2-65">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amphetamine_KEGG_ΔFosB_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Amphetamine_KEGG_ΔFosB-67">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meth_cAMP/calcium-dependent_cascade_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meth_cAMP/calcium-dependent_cascade-68">[64]</a></sup>  Phosphorylated CREB increases levels of ΔFosB, which in turn represses the <span class="nowrap">c-Fos</span> gene with the help of <a href="/wiki/Corepressor" title="Corepressor">corepressors</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler-Renthal_Figure_2_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler-Renthal_Figure_2-65">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler1_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler1-69">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler2_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler2-70">[66]</a></sup> <span class="nowrap">c-Fos</span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gene_repression" title="Gene repression">repression</a> acts as a molecular switch that enables the accumulation of ΔFosB in the neuron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-c-Fos_repression_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-c-Fos_repression-71">[67]</a></sup> A highly stable (phosphorylated) form of ΔFosB, one that persists in neurons for <span class="nowrap">1–2</span> months, slowly accumulates following repeated high-dose exposure to stimulants through this process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler1_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler1-69">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler2_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler2-70">[66]</a></sup> ΔFosB functions as "one of the master control proteins" that produces addiction-related <a href="/wiki/Neuroplasticity" title="Neuroplasticity">structural changes in the brain</a>, and upon sufficient accumulation, with the help of its downstream targets (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_factor_kappa_B" title="Nuclear factor kappa B">nuclear factor kappa B</a>), it induces an addictive state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler1_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler1-69">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler2_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler2-70">[66]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Chronic addictive drug use causes alterations in <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">gene expression</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesocorticolimbic_projection" title="Mesocorticolimbic projection">mesocorticolimbic projection</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler,_Hyman,_and_Malenka_2_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler,_Hyman,_and_Malenka_2-72">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Addiction_genetics_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Addiction_genetics-73">[69]</a></sup> The most important <a href="/wiki/Transcription_factor" title="Transcription factor">transcription factors</a> that produce these alterations are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%94FosB" title="ΔFosB">ΔFosB</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyclic_adenosine_monophosphate" title="Cyclic adenosine monophosphate">cAMP</a> response element binding protein (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CAMP_response_element_binding_protein" title="CAMP response element binding protein">CREB</a>), and nuclear factor kappa B (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_factor_kappa_B" title="Nuclear factor kappa B">NF-κB</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler-17">[16]</a></sup> ΔFosB is the most significant biomolecular mechanism in addiction because the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overexpression" title="Overexpression">overexpression</a> of ΔFosB in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D1-type" title="D1-type">D1-type</a> <a href="/wiki/Medium_spiny_neuron" title="Medium spiny neuron">medium spiny neurons</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Nucleus_accumbens" title="Nucleus accumbens">nucleus accumbens</a> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Necessary_and_sufficient" title="Necessary and sufficient">necessary and sufficient</a> for many of the neural adaptations and behavioral effects (e.g., expression-dependent increases in drug <a href="/wiki/Self-administration" title="Self-administration">self-administration</a> and <a href="#Sensitization">reward sensitization</a>) seen in drug addiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_17-5"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler-17">[16]</a></sup> ΔFosB expression in <a href="/wiki/Nucleus_accumbens" title="Nucleus accumbens">nucleus accumbens</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D1-type" title="D1-type">D1-type</a> <a href="/wiki/Medium_spiny_neuron" title="Medium spiny neuron">medium spiny neurons</a> directly and positively regulates drug <a href="/wiki/Self-administration" title="Self-administration">self-administration</a> and <a href="#Sensitization">reward sensitization</a> through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Positive_reinforcement" title="Positive reinforcement">positive reinforcement</a> while decreasing sensitivity to <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/aversion" title="wikt:aversion">aversion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ΔFosB_behaviors_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-ΔFosB_behaviors-63">[note 2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-12"><a href="#cite_note-Cellular_basis-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-What_the_ΔFosB?_61-5"><a href="#cite_note-What_the_ΔFosB?-61">[59]</a></sup> ΔFosB has been implicated in mediating addictions to many different drugs and drug classes, including <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism" title="Alcoholism">alcohol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amphetamine" title="Amphetamine">amphetamine</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Substituted_amphetamines" title="Substituted amphetamines">substituted amphetamines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cannabinoid" title="Cannabinoid">cannabinoids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cocaine" title="Cocaine">cocaine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methylphenidate" title="Methylphenidate">methylphenidate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicotine" title="Nicotine">nicotine</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opiates" title="Opiates">opiates</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenylcyclidine" title="Phenylcyclidine">phenylcyclidine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Propofol" title="Propofol">propofol</a>, among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-What_the_ΔFosB?_61-6"><a href="#cite_note-What_the_ΔFosB?-61">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_17-6"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler,_Hyman,_and_Malenka_2_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler,_Hyman,_and_Malenka_2-72">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alcoholism_ΔFosB_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alcoholism_ΔFosB-74">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MPH_ΔFosB_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-MPH_ΔFosB-75">[71]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%94JunD" title="ΔJunD">ΔJunD</a>, a transcription factor, and <a href="/wiki/EHMT2" title="EHMT2">G9a</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Histone_methyltransferase" title="Histone methyltransferase">histone methyltransferase</a>, both oppose the function of ΔFosB and inhibit increases in its expression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-13"><a href="#cite_note-Cellular_basis-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_17-7"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_2014_epigenetics_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler_2014_epigenetics-76">[72]</a></sup> Increases in nucleus accumbens ΔJunD expression (via <a href="/wiki/Viral_vector" title="Viral vector">viral vector</a>-mediated gene transfer) or G9a expression (via pharmacological means) reduces, or with a large increase can even block, many of the neural and behavioral alterations that result from chronic high-dose use of addictive drugs (i.e., the alterations mediated by ΔFosB).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G9a_reverses_ΔFosB_plasticity_62-3"><a href="#cite_note-G9a_reverses_ΔFosB_plasticity-62">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_17-8"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler-17">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>ΔFosB also plays an important role in regulating behavioral responses to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_reward" title="Natural reward">natural rewards</a>, such as palatable food, sex, and exercise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_17-9"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ΔFosB_reward_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-ΔFosB_reward-77">[73]</a></sup> Natural rewards, like drugs of abuse, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inducible_gene" title="Inducible gene">induce gene expression</a> of ΔFosB in the nucleus accumbens, and chronic acquisition of these rewards can result in a similar pathological addictive state through ΔFosB overexpression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-13"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_17-10"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ΔFosB_reward_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-ΔFosB_reward-77">[73]</a></sup> Consequently, ΔFosB is the key transcription factor involved in addictions to natural rewards (i.e., behavioral addictions) as well;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_17-11"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-14"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ΔFosB_reward_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-ΔFosB_reward-77">[73]</a></sup> in particular, ΔFosB in the nucleus accumbens is critical for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reinforcing" title="Reinforcing">reinforcing</a> effects of sexual reward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ΔFosB_reward_77-3"><a href="#cite_note-ΔFosB_reward-77">[73]</a></sup> Research on the interaction between natural and drug rewards suggests that dopaminergic psychostimulants (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Amphetamine" title="Amphetamine">amphetamine</a>) and sexual behavior act on similar biomolecular mechanisms to induce ΔFosB in the nucleus accumbens and possess bidirectional cross-<a href="/wiki/Sensitization" title="Sensitization">sensitization</a> effects that are mediated through ΔFosB.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-15"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_D1_signaling_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_D1_signaling-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_NMDA_signaling_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_NMDA_signaling-20">[19]</a></sup> This phenomenon is notable since, in humans, a <a href="/wiki/Dopamine_dysregulation_syndrome" title="Dopamine dysregulation syndrome">dopamine dysregulation syndrome</a>, characterized by drug-induced compulsive engagement in natural rewards (specifically, sexual activity, shopping, and gambling), has also been observed in some individuals taking <a href="/wiki/Dopaminergic" title="Dopaminergic">dopaminergic</a> medications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-16"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%94FosB" title="ΔFosB">ΔFosB</a> inhibitors (drugs or treatments that oppose its action) may be an effective treatment for addiction and addictive disorders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Malenka_2009_04_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Malenka_2009_04-78">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>The release of <a href="/wiki/Dopamine" title="Dopamine">dopamine</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Nucleus_accumbens" title="Nucleus accumbens">nucleus accumbens</a> plays a role in the reinforcing qualities of many forms of stimuli, including naturally reinforcing stimuli like palatable food and sex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salamone_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Salamone-79">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kauer_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kauer-80">[76]</a></sup> Altered dopamine <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmission" title="Neurotransmission">neurotransmission</a> is frequently observed following the development of an addictive state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-17"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup> In humans and lab animals that have developed an addiction, alterations in dopamine or <a href="/wiki/Opioid" title="Opioid">opioid</a> neurotransmission in the nucleus accumbens and other parts of the <a href="/wiki/Striatum" title="Striatum">striatum</a> are evident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-18"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup> Studies have found that use of certain drugs (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Cocaine" title="Cocaine">cocaine</a>) affect <a href="/wiki/Cholinergic_neuron" title="Cholinergic neuron">cholinergic neurons</a> that innervate the <a href="/wiki/Reward_system" title="Reward system">reward system</a>, in turn affecting dopamine signaling in this region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witten_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Witten-81">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reward_system">Reward system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Reward system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reward_system" title="Reward system">Reward system</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mesocorticolimbic_pathway">Mesocorticolimbic pathway</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Mesocorticolimbic pathway">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table cellpadding="0" role="presentation" style="border-spacing:0; clear:right; float:right;">
<tbody><tr>
<td><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
<div style="clear: both; font-weight: bold; font-size: 106.4%; text-align: center; background-color: aliceblue;margin-bottom:3px;">ΔFosB accumulation from excessive drug use</div>
<div style="position:relative; width:400px; height:440px; overflow:hidden; border:solid #ccc 1px; background-color:white;">
<div style="left:0px; top:0px; width:400px; position:absolute"> <a href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:%CE%94FosB_accumulation.svg" title="commons:File:ΔFosB accumulation.svg"><img alt="ΔFosB accumulation graph" data-file-height="440" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="440" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/%CE%94FosB_accumulation.svg/400px-%CE%94FosB_accumulation.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/%CE%94FosB_accumulation.svg/600px-%CE%94FosB_accumulation.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/%CE%94FosB_accumulation.svg/800px-%CE%94FosB_accumulation.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a>
</div>
<div style="text-align:center; line-height:110%;">
<div style="background-color:transparent; color:black"></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Top: this depicts the initial effects of high dose exposure to an addictive drug on <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">gene expression</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Nucleus_accumbens" title="Nucleus accumbens">nucleus accumbens</a> for various Fos family proteins (i.e., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C-Fos" title="C-Fos">c-Fos</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FosB" title="FosB">FosB</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%94FosB" title="ΔFosB">ΔFosB</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fra1" title="Fra1">Fra1</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fra2" title="Fra2">Fra2</a>).<br/>Bottom: this illustrates the progressive increase in ΔFosB expression in the nucleus accumbens following repeated twice daily drug binges, where these <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phosphorylated" title="Phosphorylated">phosphorylated</a> (35–37 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilodalton" title="Kilodalton">kilodalton</a>) ΔFosB <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isoform" title="Isoform">isoforms</a> persist in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D1-type" title="D1-type">D1-type</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medium_spiny_neurons" title="Medium spiny neurons">medium spiny neurons</a> of the nucleus accumbens for up to 2 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler2_70-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler2-70">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11572966_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11572966-82">[78]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Understanding the pathways in which drugs act and how drugs can alter those pathways is key when examining the biological basis of drug addiction. The reward pathway, known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesolimbic_system" title="Mesolimbic system">mesolimbic pathway</a>, or its extension, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesocorticolimbic_pathway" title="Mesocorticolimbic pathway">mesocorticolimbic pathway</a>, is characterized by the interaction of several areas of the brain.
</p>
<ul><li>The projections from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ventral_tegmentum" title="Ventral tegmentum">ventral tegmental area</a> (VTA) are a network of <a href="/wiki/Dopaminergic" title="Dopaminergic">dopaminergic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neurons" title="Neurons">neurons</a> with <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/colocalize" title="wikt:colocalize">co-localized</a> postsynaptic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glutamate" title="Glutamate">glutamate</a> receptors (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AMPAR" title="AMPAR">AMPAR</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NMDAR" title="NMDAR">NMDAR</a>). These cells respond when stimuli indicative of a reward are present. The VTA supports learning and sensitization development and releases DA into the <a href="/wiki/Forebrain" title="Forebrain">forebrain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones_and_Bonci_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jones_and_Bonci-83">[79]</a></sup> These neurons also project and release DA into the nucleus accumbens,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eisch_and_Harburg_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eisch_and_Harburg-84">[80]</a></sup> through the <a href="/wiki/Mesolimbic_pathway" title="Mesolimbic pathway">mesolimbic pathway</a>. Virtually all drugs causing drug addiction increase the dopamine release in the mesolimbic pathway,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rang_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rang-85">[81]</a></sup> in addition to their specific effects.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Nucleus_accumbens" title="Nucleus accumbens">nucleus accumbens</a> (NAcc) is one output of the VTA projections. The nucleus accumbens itself consists mainly of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GABA" title="GABA">GABAergic</a> <a href="/wiki/Medium_spiny_neuron" title="Medium spiny neuron">medium spiny neurons</a> (MSNs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kourrich_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kourrich-86">[82]</a></sup> The NAcc is associated with acquiring and eliciting conditioned behaviors, and is involved in the increased sensitivity to drugs as addiction progresses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones_and_Bonci_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jones_and_Bonci-83">[79]</a></sup> Overexpression of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%94FosB" title="ΔFosB">ΔFosB</a> in the nucleus accumbens is a necessary common factor in essentially all known forms of addiction;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-14"><a href="#cite_note-Cellular_basis-3">[3]</a></sup> ΔFosB is a strong positive modulator of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Positively_reinforced" title="Positively reinforced">positively reinforced</a> behaviors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-15"><a href="#cite_note-Cellular_basis-3">[3]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Prefrontal_cortex" title="Prefrontal cortex">prefrontal cortex</a>, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anterior_cingulate" title="Anterior cingulate">anterior cingulate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orbitofrontal_cortex" title="Orbitofrontal cortex">orbitofrontal</a> cortices,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalivas_and_Volkow_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kalivas_and_Volkow-87">[83]</a></sup> is another VTA output in the mesocorticolimbic pathway; it is important for the integration of information which helps determine whether a behavior will be elicited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Floresco_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Floresco-88">[84]</a></sup> It is also critical for forming associations between the rewarding experience of drug use and cues in the environment. Importantly, these cues are strong mediators of drug-seeking behavior and can trigger relapse even after months or years of abstinence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[85]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Other brain structures that are involved in addiction include:
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Basolateral_amygdala" title="Basolateral amygdala">basolateral amygdala</a> projects into the NAcc and is thought to also be important for motivation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Floresco_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Floresco-88">[84]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Hippocampus" title="Hippocampus">hippocampus</a> is involved in drug addiction, because of its role in learning and memory. Much of this evidence stems from investigations showing that manipulating cells in the hippocampus alters dopamine levels in NAcc and firing rates of VTA dopaminergic cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eisch_and_Harburg_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eisch_and_Harburg-84">[80]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_dopamine_and_glutamate">Role of dopamine and glutamate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Role of dopamine and glutamate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Dopamine is the primary neurotransmitter of the reward system in the brain. It plays a role in regulating movement, emotion, cognition, motivation, and feelings of pleasure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arch_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-arch-90">[86]</a></sup> Natural rewards, like eating, as well as recreational drug use cause a release of dopamine, and are associated with the reinforcing nature of these stimuli.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arch_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-arch-90">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drugs-brain_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-drugs-brain-91">[87]</a></sup> Nearly all addictive drugs, directly or indirectly, act upon the brain's reward system by heightening dopaminergic activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-addict_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-addict-92">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>Excessive intake of many types of addictive drugs results in repeated release of high amounts of dopamine, which in turn affects the reward pathway directly through heightened <a href="/wiki/Dopamine_receptor" title="Dopamine receptor">dopamine receptor</a> activation. Prolonged and abnormally high levels of dopamine in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synaptic_cleft" title="Synaptic cleft">synaptic cleft</a> can induce receptor <a href="/wiki/Downregulation_and_upregulation" title="Downregulation and upregulation">downregulation</a> in the neural pathway. Downregulation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesolimbic" title="Mesolimbic">mesolimbic</a> dopamine receptors can result in a decrease in the sensitivity to natural reinforcers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arch_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-arch-90">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>Drug seeking behavior is induced by glutamatergic projections from the prefrontal cortex to the nucleus accumbens. This idea is supported with data from experiments showing that drug seeking behavior can be prevented following the inhibition of <a href="/wiki/AMPA" title="AMPA">AMPA</a> glutamate receptors and glutamate release in the nucleus accumbens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalivas_and_Volkow_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kalivas_and_Volkow-87">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reward_sensitization">Reward sensitization<span class="anchor" id="Sensitization"></span><span class="anchor" id="Drug_sensitization"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Reward sensitization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center; float:right; margin-left:1em;">

</td></tr>
<tr>
<td scope="row" style="height: 40px"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cdk5" title="Cdk5">Cdk5</a></td>
<td>↑</td>
<td style="text-align:left"> • <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GluR1" title="GluR1">GluR1</a> synaptic protein phosphorylation<br/> • Expansion of <abbr title="nucleus accumbens">NAcc</abbr> dendritic processes</td>
<td>Decreased drug reward<br/><small>(net effect)</small>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Reward sensitization</b> is a process that causes an increase in the amount of reward (specifically, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Incentive_salience" title="Incentive salience">incentive salience</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[note 5]</a></sup>) that is assigned by the brain to a rewarding stimulus (e.g., a drug). In simple terms, when reward sensitization to a specific stimulus (e.g., a drug) occurs, an individual's "wanting" or desire for the stimulus itself and its associated <a href="/wiki/Cue_reactivity" title="Cue reactivity">cues</a> increases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAcc_function_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-NAcc_function-97">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Incentive_salience_and_motivation_review_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-Incentive_salience_and_motivation_review-96">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reinforcement_in_addiction_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reinforcement_in_addiction-99">[92]</a></sup> Reward sensitization normally occurs following chronically high levels of exposure to the stimulus. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%94FosB" title="ΔFosB">ΔFosB</a> (DeltaFosB) expression in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D1-type" title="D1-type">D1-type</a> <a href="/wiki/Medium_spiny_neuron" title="Medium spiny neuron">medium spiny neurons</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Nucleus_accumbens" title="Nucleus accumbens">nucleus accumbens</a> has been shown to directly and positively regulate reward sensitization involving drugs and natural rewards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-18"><a href="#cite_note-Cellular_basis-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-What_the_ΔFosB?_61-9"><a href="#cite_note-What_the_ΔFosB?-61">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G9a_reverses_ΔFosB_plasticity_62-4"><a href="#cite_note-G9a_reverses_ΔFosB_plasticity-62">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>"Cue-induced wanting" or "cue-triggered wanting", a form of craving that occurs in addiction, is responsible for most of the compulsive behavior that people with addictions exhibit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Incentive_salience_and_motivation_review_96-3"><a href="#cite_note-Incentive_salience_and_motivation_review-96">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reinforcement_in_addiction_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-Reinforcement_in_addiction-99">[92]</a></sup> During the development of an addiction, the repeated association of otherwise neutral and even non-rewarding <a href="/wiki/Stimulation" title="Stimulation">stimuli</a> with drug consumption triggers an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Associative_learning" title="Associative learning">associative learning</a> process that causes these previously neutral stimuli to act as <a href="/wiki/Reinforcement#Secondary_reinforcers" title="Reinforcement">conditioned positive reinforcers</a> of addictive drug use (i.e., these stimuli start to function as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drug_cues" title="Drug cues">drug cues</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Incentive_salience_and_motivation_review_96-4"><a href="#cite_note-Incentive_salience_and_motivation_review-96">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pleasure_system_-_incentive_sensitization_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pleasure_system_-_incentive_sensitization-100">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reinforcement_in_addiction_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-Reinforcement_in_addiction-99">[92]</a></sup> As conditioned positive reinforcers of drug use, these previously neutral stimuli are assigned incentive salience (which manifests as a craving) – sometimes at pathologically high levels due to reward sensitization – which can <a href="/wiki/Pavlovian-instrumental_transfer" title="Pavlovian-instrumental transfer">transfer to the primary reinforcer</a> (e.g., the use of an addictive drug) with which it was originally paired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Incentive_salience_and_motivation_review_96-5"><a href="#cite_note-Incentive_salience_and_motivation_review-96">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pleasure_system_-_incentive_sensitization_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pleasure_system_-_incentive_sensitization-100">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reinforcement_in_addiction_99-3"><a href="#cite_note-Reinforcement_in_addiction-99">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Research on the interaction between natural and drug rewards suggests that dopaminergic psychostimulants (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Amphetamine" title="Amphetamine">amphetamine</a>) and sexual behavior act on similar biomolecular mechanisms to induce ΔFosB in the nucleus accumbens and possess a bidirectional <b>reward cross-sensitization</b> effect<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[note 6]</a></sup> that is mediated through ΔFosB.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-19"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_D1_signaling_19-4"><a href="#cite_note-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_D1_signaling-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_NMDA_signaling_20-4"><a href="#cite_note-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_NMDA_signaling-20">[19]</a></sup> In contrast to ΔFosB's reward-sensitizing effect, <a href="/wiki/CREB" title="CREB">CREB</a> transcriptional activity decreases user's sensitivity to the rewarding effects of the substance. CREB transcription in the nucleus accumbens is implicated in <a href="/wiki/Psychological_dependence" title="Psychological dependence">psychological dependence</a> and symptoms involving a <a href="/wiki/Anhedonia" title="Anhedonia">lack of pleasure or motivation</a> during <a href="/wiki/Drug_withdrawal" title="Drug withdrawal">drug withdrawal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-19"><a href="#cite_note-Cellular_basis-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11572966_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11572966-82">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18640924_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18640924-93">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
