<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neuroepigenetic_mechanisms">Neuroepigenetic mechanisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Neuroepigenetic mechanisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Neuroepigenetics" title="Neuroepigenetics">Neuroepigenetics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chromatin_remodeling" title="Chromatin remodeling">Chromatin remodeling</a></div>
<p>Altered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epigenetic" title="Epigenetic">epigenetic</a> regulation of <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">gene expression</a> within the brain's reward system plays a significant and complex role in the development of drug addiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_2014_epigenetics_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler_2014_epigenetics-76">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chromatin_states_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chromatin_states-102">[94]</a></sup> Addictive drugs are associated with three types of epigenetic modifications within neurons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_2014_epigenetics_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler_2014_epigenetics-76">[72]</a></sup> These are (1) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Histone_modification" title="Histone modification">histone modifications</a>, (2) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epigenetic_methylation" title="Epigenetic methylation">epigenetic methylation</a> of DNA at <a href="/wiki/CpG_site" title="CpG site">CpG sites</a> at (or adjacent to) particular genes, and (3) epigenetic <a href="/wiki/Downregulation_and_upregulation" title="Downregulation and upregulation">downregulation or upregulation</a> of <a href="/wiki/MicroRNA" title="MicroRNA">microRNAs</a> which have particular target genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_2014_epigenetics_76-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler_2014_epigenetics-76">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_17-12"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chromatin_states_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chromatin_states-102">[94]</a></sup> As an example, while hundreds of genes in the cells of the nucleus accumbens (NAc) exhibit histone modifications following drug exposure – particularly, altered acetylation and methylation states of <a href="/wiki/Histone" title="Histone">histone</a> <a href="/wiki/Residue_(chemistry)#Biochemistry" title="Residue (chemistry)">residues</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chromatin_states_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chromatin_states-102">[94]</a></sup> – most other genes in the NAc cells do not show such changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_2014_epigenetics_76-4"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler_2014_epigenetics-76">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Diagnosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Substance_use_disorder#Diagnosis" title="Substance use disorder">Substance use disorder § Diagnosis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Problem_gambling#Diagnosis" title="Problem gambling">Problem gambling § Diagnosis</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="DSM-5">DSM-5</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: DSM-5">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The 5th edition of the <a href="/wiki/Diagnostic_and_Statistical_Manual_of_Mental_Disorders" title="Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders">Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders</a> (DSM-5) uses the term "<a href="/wiki/Substance_use_disorder" title="Substance use disorder">substance use disorder</a>" to refer to a spectrum of drug use-related disorders. The DSM-5 eliminates the terms "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drug_abuse" title="Drug abuse">abuse</a>" and "dependence" from diagnostic categories, instead using the specifiers of <i>mild</i>, <i>moderate</i> and <i>severe</i> to indicate the extent of disordered use. These specifiers are determined by the number of diagnostic criteria present in a given case. In the DSM-5, the term <i>drug addiction</i> is synonymous with <i>severe substance use disorder</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Surgeon_General_severe_SUD_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Surgeon_General_severe_SUD-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brain_disease_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Brain_disease-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>The DSM-5 introduced a new diagnostic category for behavioral addictions; however, <a href="/wiki/Problem_gambling" title="Problem gambling">problem gambling</a> is the only condition included in that category in the 5th edition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Addiction-dependence_distinction_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Addiction-dependence_distinction-14">[13]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internet_gaming_disorder" title="Internet gaming disorder">Internet gaming disorder</a> is listed as a "condition requiring further study" in the DSM-5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>Past editions have used <a href="/wiki/Physical_dependence" title="Physical dependence">physical dependence</a> and the associated withdrawal syndrome to identify an addictive state. <a href="/wiki/Physical_dependence" title="Physical dependence">Physical dependence</a> occurs when the body has adjusted by incorporating the substance into its "normal" functioning – i.e., attains <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostasis</a> – and therefore physical withdrawal symptoms occur upon cessation of use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid10511013_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid10511013-104">[96]</a></sup> Tolerance is the process by which the body continually adapts to the substance and requires increasingly larger amounts to achieve the original effects. Withdrawal refers to physical and psychological symptoms experienced when reducing or discontinuing a substance that the body has become dependent on. Symptoms of withdrawal generally include but are not limited to body aches, <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">anxiety</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irritability" title="Irritability">irritability</a>, intense <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Craving_(withdrawal)" title="Craving (withdrawal)">cravings</a> for the substance, <a href="/wiki/Nausea" title="Nausea">nausea</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hallucinations" title="Hallucinations">hallucinations</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Headaches" title="Headaches">headaches</a>, cold sweats, <a href="/wiki/Tremor" title="Tremor">tremors</a>, and seizures.
</p><p>Medical researchers who actively study addiction have criticized the DSM classification of addiction for being flawed and involving arbitrary diagnostic criteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHMH_3e_terms-DSM_flaw_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-NHMH_3e_terms-DSM_flaw-15">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="ICD-11">ICD-11</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: ICD-11">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The eleventh revision of the <a href="/wiki/International_Classification_of_Diseases" title="International Classification of Diseases">International Classification of Diseases</a>, commonly referred to as <a href="/wiki/ICD-11" title="ICD-11">ICD-11</a>, conceptualizes diagnosis somewhat differently. ICD-11 first distinguishes between problems with psychoactive substance use ("Disorders due to substance use") and behavioral addictions ("Disorders due to addictive behaviours").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICD-11_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-ICD-11-105">[97]</a></sup> With regard to psychoactive substances, ICD-11 explains that the included substances initially produce "pleasant or appealing psychoactive effects that are rewarding and reinforcing with repeated use, [but] with continued use, many of the included substances have the capacity to produce dependence. They also have the potential to cause numerous forms of harm, both to mental and physical health."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-substance-disorder_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-substance-disorder-106">[98]</a></sup> Instead of the DSM-5 approach of one diagnosis ("Substance Use Disorder") covering all types of problematic substance use, ICD-11 offers three diagnostic possibilities: 1) Episode of Harmful Psychoactive Substance Use, 2) Harmful Pattern of Psychoactive Substance Use, and 3) Substance Dependence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-substance-disorder_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-substance-disorder-106">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Prevention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abuse_liability">Abuse liability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Abuse liability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Abuse liability, which is also known as addiction liability, is the tendency to use drugs in a non-medical situation. This is typically for euphoria, mood changing, or sedation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-107">[99]</a></sup> Abuse liability is used when the person using the drugs wants something that they otherwise can not obtain. The only way to obtain this is through the use of drugs. When looking at abuse liability there are a number of determining factors in whether the drug is abused. These factors are: the chemical makeup of the drug, the effects on the brain, and the age, vulnerability, and the health (mental and physical) of the population being studied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-107">[99]</a></sup> There are a few drugs with a specific chemical makeup that leads to a high abuse liability. These are: cocaine, heroin, inhalants, marijuana, MDMA (ecstasy), methamphetamine, PCP, synthetic cannabinoids, synthetic cathinones (bath salts), nicotine (e.g. tobacco), and alcohol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment_and_management">Treatment and management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Treatment and management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Addiction_recovery_groups" title="Addiction recovery groups">Addiction recovery groups</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_behavioral_therapy" title="Cognitive behavioral therapy">Cognitive behavioral therapy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Drug_rehabilitation" title="Drug rehabilitation">Drug rehabilitation</a></div>
<p>According to a review, "in order to be effective, all pharmacological or biologically based treatments for addiction need to be integrated into other established forms of addiction rehabilitation, such as cognitive behavioral therapy, individual and group psychotherapy, <a href="/wiki/Behavior_modification" title="Behavior modification">behavior modification</a> strategies, <a href="/wiki/Twelve-step_program" title="Twelve-step program">twelve-step programs</a>, and residential treatment facilities."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reward_system_and_psychostimulants_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reward_system_and_psychostimulants-109">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>A biosocial approach to the treatment of addiction brings to the fore the social determinants of illness and wellbeing and considers the dynamic and reciprocal relationships that exist for, and influence, the individual's experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>The work of A.V. Schlosser (2018) aims to pronounce the individual lived experiences of women receiving medication-assisted treatment (e.g., methadone, naltrexone, burprenorphine) in a long-term rehabilitation setting, through a twenty month long ethnographic fieldwork investigation. This person-centred research shows how the experiences of these women "emerge from stable systems of inequality based in intersectional gender, race, and class marginalization entangled with processes of intra-action."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schlosser_188–207_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schlosser_188–207-111">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Viewing addiction treatment through this lens also highlights the importance of framing clients' own bodies as "social flesh." As Schlosser (2018) points out, "client bodies" as well as the "embodied experiences of self and social belonging emerge in and through the structures, temporalities, and expectations of the treatment centre."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schlosser_188–207_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schlosser_188–207-111">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Further challenges and embodied tensions have the potential to arise as a result of the dynamics inherent in the patient-provider relationship, in addition to the experience of being "alienated from their own bodies, psyches and sociality's when sedated on medications in treatment."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schlosser_188–207_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schlosser_188–207-111">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Biotechnologies currently make up a large portion of the future treatments for addiction. To name a few, deep-brain stimulation, agonist/antagonist implants and hapten conjugate vaccines. Vaccinations against addiction specifically overlaps with the belief that memory plays a large role in the damaging effects of addiction and relapses. Hapten conjugate vaccines are designed to block opioid receptors in one area, while allowing other receptors to behave normally. Essentially, once a high can no longer be achieved in relation to a traumatic event, the relation of drugs to a traumatic memory can be disconnected and therapy can then play a role in treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavioral_therapy">Behavioral therapy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Behavioral therapy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A meta-analytic review on the efficacy of various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Behavioral_therapy" title="Behavioral therapy">behavioral therapies</a> for treating drug and behavioral addictions found that <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_behavioral_therapy" title="Cognitive behavioral therapy">cognitive behavioral therapy</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Relapse_prevention" title="Relapse prevention">relapse prevention</a> and <a href="/wiki/Contingency_management" title="Contingency management">contingency management</a>), <a href="/wiki/Motivational_interviewing" title="Motivational interviewing">motivational interviewing</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Community_reinforcement_approach_and_family_training" title="Community reinforcement approach and family training">community reinforcement approach</a> were effective interventions with moderate effect sizes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-German_meta-analysis_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-German_meta-analysis-113">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Clinical and preclinical evidence indicate that consistent aerobic exercise, especially endurance exercise (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marathon_running" title="Marathon running">marathon running</a>), actually prevents the development of certain drug addictions and is an effective adjunct treatment for drug addiction, and for psychostimulant addiction in particular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-33"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Addiction_review_2016_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Addiction_review_2016-114">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Running_vs_addiction_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Running_vs_addiction-115">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Exercise_Rev_3_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Exercise_Rev_3-116">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Exercise,_addiction_prevention,_and_ΔFosB_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Exercise,_addiction_prevention,_and_ΔFosB-117">[109]</a></sup> Consistent aerobic exercise magnitude-dependently (i.e., by duration and intensity) reduces drug addiction risk, which appears to occur through the reversal of drug induced addiction-related neuroplasticity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-34"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Running_vs_addiction_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-Running_vs_addiction-115">[107]</a></sup> One review noted that exercise may prevent the development of drug addiction by altering <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%94FosB" title="ΔFosB">ΔFosB</a> or <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C-Fos" title="C-Fos">c-Fos</a></span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunoreactivity" title="Immunoreactivity">immunoreactivity</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Striatum" title="Striatum">striatum</a> or other parts of the <a href="/wiki/Reward_system" title="Reward system">reward system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Exercise,_addiction_prevention,_and_ΔFosB_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Exercise,_addiction_prevention,_and_ΔFosB-117">[109]</a></sup> Aerobic exercise decreases drug self-administration, reduces the likelihood of relapse, and induces opposite effects on <a href="/wiki/Striatum" title="Striatum">striatal</a> <a href="/wiki/Dopamine_receptor_D2" title="Dopamine receptor D2">dopamine receptor D<sub>2</sub></a> (DRD2) signaling (increased DRD2 density) to those induced by addictions to several drug classes (decreased DRD2 density).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-35"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Running_vs_addiction_115-2"><a href="#cite_note-Running_vs_addiction-115">[107]</a></sup> Consequently, consistent aerobic exercise may lead to better treatment outcomes when used as an adjunct treatment for drug addiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-36"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Running_vs_addiction_115-3"><a href="#cite_note-Running_vs_addiction-115">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Exercise_Rev_3_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-Exercise_Rev_3-116">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medication">Medication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Medication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alcohol_addiction">Alcohol addiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Alcohol addiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism" title="Alcoholism">Alcoholism</a></div>
<p>Alcohol, like opioids, can induce a severe state of <a href="/wiki/Physical_dependence" title="Physical dependence">physical dependence</a> and produce withdrawal symptoms such as <a href="/wiki/Delirium_tremens" title="Delirium tremens">delirium tremens</a>. Because of this, treatment for alcohol addiction usually involves a combined approach dealing with dependence and addiction simultaneously. Benzodiazepines have the largest and the best evidence base in the treatment of alcohol withdrawal and are considered the gold standard of <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_detoxification" title="Alcohol detoxification">alcohol detoxification</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pharmacological treatments for alcohol addiction include drugs like <a href="/wiki/Naltrexone" title="Naltrexone">naltrexone</a> (opioid antagonist), <a href="/wiki/Disulfiram" title="Disulfiram">disulfiram</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acamprosate" title="Acamprosate">acamprosate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Topiramate" title="Topiramate">topiramate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[112]</a></sup> Rather than substituting for alcohol, these drugs are intended to affect the desire to drink, either by directly reducing cravings as with acamprosate and topiramate, or by producing unpleasant effects when alcohol is consumed, as with disulfiram. These drugs can be effective if treatment is maintained, but compliance can be an issue as alcoholic patients often forget to take their medication, or discontinue use because of excessive side effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[114]</a></sup> According to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cochrane_Collaboration" title="Cochrane Collaboration">Cochrane Collaboration</a> review, the opioid antagonist <a href="/wiki/Naltrexone" title="Naltrexone">naltrexone</a> has been shown to be an effective treatment for alcoholism, with the effects lasting three to twelve months after the end of treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavioral_addictions">Behavioral addictions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Behavioral addictions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable relarticle mainarticle selfreference noprint" role="note">This section is <a href="/wiki/Help:Transclusion" title="Help:Transclusion">transcluded</a> from <a href="/wiki/Behavioral_addiction" title="Behavioral addiction">Behavioral addiction</a>. <span class="plainlinks" style="font-style: normal; font-size:85%;">(<a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Behavioral_addiction&amp;action=edit">edit</a> | <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Behavioral_addiction&amp;action=history">history</a>)</span></div>
<p>Behavioral addiction is a treatable condition. Treatment options include <a href="/wiki/Psychotherapy" title="Psychotherapy">psychotherapy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psychopharmacology" title="Psychopharmacology">psychopharmacotherapy</a> (i.e., medications) or a combination of both. <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_behavioral_therapy" title="Cognitive behavioral therapy">Cognitive behavioral therapy</a> (CBT) is the most common form of psychotherapy used in treating behavioral addictions; it focuses on identifying patterns that trigger <a href="/wiki/Compulsive_behavior" title="Compulsive behavior">compulsive behavior</a> and making lifestyle changes to promote healthier behaviors. Because cognitive behavioral therapy is considered a short term therapy, the number of sessions for treatment normally ranges from five to twenty. During the session, therapists will lead patients through the topics of identifying the issue, becoming aware of one's thoughts surrounding the issue, identifying any negative or false thinking, and reshaping said negative and false thinking. While CBT does not cure behavioral addiction, it does help with coping with the condition in a healthy way. Currently, there are no medications approved for treatment of behavioral addictions in general, but some medications used for treatment of drug addiction may also be beneficial with specific behavioral addictions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Behavioral_addictions_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-Behavioral_addictions-23">[22]</a></sup> Any unrelated psychiatric disorders should be kept under control, and differentiated from the contributing factors that cause the addiction.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cannabinoid_addiction">Cannabinoid addiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Cannabinoid addiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>As of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there are no effective pharmacological interventions for cannabinoid addiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marijuana_addiction_2010_review_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marijuana_addiction_2010_review-124">[116]</a></sup> A 2013 review on cannabinoid addiction noted that the development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CB1_receptor" title="CB1 receptor">CB1 receptor</a> agonists that have reduced interaction with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%92-arrestin_2" title="Β-arrestin 2">β-arrestin 2</a> signaling might be therapeutically useful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cannabinoid_addiction_2013_review_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cannabinoid_addiction_2013_review-125">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nicotine_addiction">Nicotine addiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Nicotine addiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Smoking_cessation" title="Smoking cessation">Smoking cessation</a></div>
<p>Another area in which drug treatment has been widely used is in the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Nicotine" title="Nicotine">nicotine</a> addiction, which usually involves the use of <a href="/wiki/Nicotine_replacement_therapy" title="Nicotine replacement therapy">nicotine replacement therapy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicotinic_receptor_antagonist" title="Nicotinic receptor antagonist">nicotinic receptor antagonists</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicotinic_receptor" title="Nicotinic receptor">nicotinic receptor</a> <a href="/wiki/Partial_agonist" title="Partial agonist">partial agonists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nicotine_addiction_therapies_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-nicotine_addiction_therapies-126">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid24484986_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid24484986-127">[119]</a></sup> Examples of drugs that act on nicotinic receptors and have been used for treating nicotine addiction include antagonists like <a href="/wiki/Bupropion" title="Bupropion">bupropion</a> and the partial agonist <a href="/wiki/Varenicline" title="Varenicline">varenicline</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nicotine_addiction_therapies_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-nicotine_addiction_therapies-126">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid24484986_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid24484986-127">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Opioid_addiction">Opioid addiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Opioid addiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Opioid_use_disorder" title="Opioid use disorder">Opioid use disorder</a></div>
<p>Opioids cause <a href="/wiki/Physical_dependence" title="Physical dependence">physical dependence</a>, and treatment typically addresses both dependence and addiction.
</p><p>Physical dependence is treated using replacement drugs such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suboxone" title="Suboxone">suboxone</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subutex" title="Subutex">subutex</a> (both containing the active ingredients <a href="/wiki/Buprenorphine" title="Buprenorphine">buprenorphine</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Methadone" title="Methadone">methadone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[121]</a></sup> Although these drugs perpetuate physical dependence, the goal of opiate maintenance is to provide a measure of control over both pain and cravings. Use of replacement drugs increases the addicted individual's ability to function normally and eliminates the negative consequences of obtaining controlled substances illicitly. Once a prescribed dosage is stabilized, treatment enters maintenance or tapering phases. In the United States, opiate replacement therapy is tightly regulated in <a href="/wiki/Methadone_clinic" title="Methadone clinic">methadone clinics</a> and under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DATA_2000" title="DATA 2000">DATA 2000</a> legislation. In some countries, other opioid derivatives such as <a href="/wiki/Dihydrocodeine" title="Dihydrocodeine">dihydrocodeine</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[122]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dihydroetorphine" title="Dihydroetorphine">dihydroetorphine</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[123]</a></sup> and even <a href="/wiki/Heroin" title="Heroin">heroin</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Metrebian1_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Metrebian1-132">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Metrebian2_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Metrebian2-133">[125]</a></sup> are used as substitute drugs for illegal street opiates, with different prescriptions being given depending on the needs of the individual patient. <a href="/wiki/Baclofen" title="Baclofen">Baclofen</a> has led to successful reductions of cravings for stimulants, alcohol, and opioids, and also alleviates <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_withdrawal_syndrome" title="Alcohol withdrawal syndrome">alcohol withdrawal syndrome</a>. Many patients have stated they "became indifferent to alcohol" or "indifferent to cocaine" overnight after starting baclofen therapy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kenna_GA,_Nielsen_DM,_Mello_P,_Schiesl_A,_Swift_RM_2007_213–37_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kenna_GA,_Nielsen_DM,_Mello_P,_Schiesl_A,_Swift_RM_2007_213–37-134">[126]</a></sup> Some studies show the interconnection between opioid <a href="/wiki/Drug_detoxification" title="Drug detoxification">drug detoxification</a> and overdose mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12727768_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12727768-135">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychostimulant_addiction">Psychostimulant addiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Psychostimulant addiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>As of May 2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there is no effective <a href="/wiki/Pharmacotherapy" title="Pharmacotherapy">pharmacotherapy</a> for any form of psychostimulant addiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reward_system_and_psychostimulants_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Reward_system_and_psychostimulants-109">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid24716825_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid24716825-136">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cochrane_2013_treatments_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cochrane_2013_treatments-137">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23039267_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid23039267-138">[130]</a></sup> Reviews from 2015, 2016, and 2018 indicated that <a href="/wiki/TAAR1" title="TAAR1">TAAR1</a>-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selective_agonist" title="Selective agonist">selective agonists</a> have significant therapeutic potential as a treatment for psychostimulant addictions;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller+Grandy_2016_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miller+Grandy_2016-139">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TAAR1_addiction_2015_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-TAAR1_addiction_2015-140">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curable_brain_disorder?_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curable_brain_disorder?-141">[133]</a></sup> however, as of 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the only compounds which are known to function as TAAR1-selective agonists are <a href="/wiki/Experimental_drug" title="Experimental drug">experimental drugs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller+Grandy_2016_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-Miller+Grandy_2016-139">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TAAR1_addiction_2015_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-TAAR1_addiction_2015-140">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curable_brain_disorder?_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-Curable_brain_disorder?-141">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Research indicates that vaccines which utilize anti-drug <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monoclonal_antibodies" title="Monoclonal antibodies">monoclonal antibodies</a> can mitigate drug-induced positive reinforcement by preventing the drug from moving across the <a href="/wiki/Blood%E2%80%93brain_barrier" title="Blood–brain barrier">blood–brain barrier</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaccine-antibody_immunotherapy_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vaccine-antibody_immunotherapy-142">[134]</a></sup> however, current vaccine-based therapies are only effective in a relatively small subset of individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaccine-antibody_immunotherapy_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vaccine-antibody_immunotherapy-142">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Primary_source:_Antibody_level_limitation_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Primary_source:_Antibody_level_limitation-143">[135]</a></sup> As of November 2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, vaccine-based therapies are being tested in human clinical trials as a treatment for addiction and preventive measure against drug overdoses involving nicotine, cocaine, and methamphetamine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaccine-antibody_immunotherapy_142-2"><a href="#cite_note-Vaccine-antibody_immunotherapy-142">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>The new study shows, that the vaccine may also save lives during a <a href="/wiki/Drug_overdose" title="Drug overdose">drug overdose</a>. In this instance, the idea is that the body will respond to the vaccine by quickly producing antibodies to prevent the opioids from accessing the brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since addiction involves abnormalities in <a href="/wiki/Glutamate_(neurotransmitter)" title="Glutamate (neurotransmitter)">glutamate</a> and <a href="/wiki/GABAergic" title="GABAergic">GABAergic</a> neurotransmission,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATS_pharmacotherapies_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-ATS_pharmacotherapies-145">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glutamate_and_GABA_systems_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glutamate_and_GABA_systems-146">[138]</a></sup> receptors associated with these neurotransmitters (e.g., <a href="/wiki/AMPA_receptor" title="AMPA receptor">AMPA receptors</a>, <a href="/wiki/NMDA_receptor" title="NMDA receptor">NMDA receptors</a>, and <a href="/wiki/GABAB_receptor" title="GABAB receptor">GABA<sub>B</sub> receptors</a>) are potential therapeutic targets for addictions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATS_pharmacotherapies_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-ATS_pharmacotherapies-145">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glutamate_and_GABA_systems_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-Glutamate_and_GABA_systems-146">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GABAB_PAMs_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-GABAB_PAMs-147">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GABAB_PAMs_2_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-GABAB_PAMs_2-148">[140]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N-acetylcysteine" title="N-acetylcysteine">N-acetylcysteine</a>, which affects <a href="/wiki/Metabotropic_glutamate_receptor" title="Metabotropic glutamate receptor">metabotropic glutamate receptors</a> and NMDA receptors, has shown some benefit in preclinical and clinical studies involving addictions to cocaine, heroin, and cannabinoids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATS_pharmacotherapies_145-2"><a href="#cite_note-ATS_pharmacotherapies-145">[137]</a></sup> It may also be useful as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adjunct_therapy" title="Adjunct therapy">adjunct therapy</a> for addictions to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphetamine-type_stimulants" title="Amphetamine-type stimulants">amphetamine-type stimulants</a>, but more clinical research is required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATS_pharmacotherapies_145-3"><a href="#cite_note-ATS_pharmacotherapies-145">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>Current medical reviews of research involving lab animals have identified a drug class – class I <a href="/wiki/Histone_deacetylase_inhibitor" title="Histone deacetylase inhibitor">histone deacetylase inhibitors</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[note 7]</a></sup> – that indirectly inhibits the function and further increases in the expression of accumbal ΔFosB by inducing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G9a" title="G9a">G9a</a> expression in the nucleus accumbens after prolonged use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G9a_reverses_ΔFosB_plasticity_62-5"><a href="#cite_note-G9a_reverses_ΔFosB_plasticity-62">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_2014_epigenetics_76-5"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler_2014_epigenetics-76">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amphetamine_epigenetics_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-Amphetamine_epigenetics-149">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chromatin_states_102-4"><a href="#cite_note-Chromatin_states-102">[94]</a></sup> These reviews and subsequent preliminary evidence which used <a href="/wiki/Oral_administration" title="Oral administration">oral administration</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intraperitoneal_administration" title="Intraperitoneal administration">intraperitoneal administration</a> of the sodium salt of <a href="/wiki/Butyric_acid" title="Butyric acid">butyric acid</a> or other class I HDAC inhibitors for an extended period indicate that these drugs have efficacy in reducing addictive behavior in lab animals<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[note 8]</a></sup> that have developed addictions to ethanol, psychostimulants (i.e., amphetamine and cocaine), nicotine, and opiates;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_2014_epigenetics_76-7"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler_2014_epigenetics-76">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chromatin_states_102-6"><a href="#cite_note-Chromatin_states-102">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HDACi_primaries_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-HDACi_primaries-151">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HDAC_alcoholism_review_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-HDAC_alcoholism_review-153">[143]</a></sup> however, few clinical trials involving humans with addictions and any HDAC class I inhibitors have been conducted to test for treatment efficacy in humans or identify an optimal dosing regimen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[note 9]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gene_therapy" title="Gene therapy">Gene therapy</a> for addiction is an active area of research. One line of gene therapy research involves the use of <a href="/wiki/Viral_vector" title="Viral vector">viral vectors</a> to increase the expression of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dopamine_D2_receptor" title="Dopamine D2 receptor">dopamine D2 receptor</a> proteins in the brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Epidemiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Due to cultural variations, the proportion of individuals who develop a drug or behavioral addiction within a specified time period (i.e., the <a href="/wiki/Prevalence" title="Prevalence">prevalence</a>) varies over time, by country, and across national <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demographics" title="Demographics">population demographics</a> (e.g., by age group, socioeconomic status, etc.).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction_33-15"><a href="#cite_note-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction-33">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The prevalence of alcohol dependence is not as high as is seen in other regions. In Asia, not only socioeconomic factors but also biological factors influence drinking behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>The overall prevalence of smartphone ownership is 62%, ranging from 41% in China to 84% in South Korea. Moreover, participation in online gaming ranges from 11% in China to 39% in Japan. Hong Kong has the highest number of adolescents reporting daily or above Internet use (68%). <a href="/wiki/Internet_addiction_disorder" title="Internet addiction disorder">Internet addiction disorder</a> is highest in the Philippines, according to both the IAT (Internet Addiction Test) – 5% and the CIAS-R (Revised Chen Internet Addiction Scale) – 21%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The prevalence of substance use disorder among Australians was reported at 5.1% in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2015, the estimated prevalence among the adult population was 18.4% for heavy episodic alcohol use (in the past 30 days); 15.2% for daily tobacco smoking; and 3.8, 0.77, 0.37 and 0.35% in 2017 cannabis, amphetamine, opioid and cocaine use. The mortality rates for alcohol and illicit drugs were highest in Eastern Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Based upon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Representative_sample" title="Representative sample">representative samples</a> of the US youth population in 2011, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lifetime_prevalence" title="Lifetime prevalence">lifetime prevalence</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[note 10]</a></sup> of addictions to alcohol and illicit drugs has been estimated to be approximately 8% and 2–3% respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Epidem_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Epidem-166">[154]</a></sup> Based upon representative samples of the US adult population in 2011, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Period_prevalence" title="Period prevalence">12 month prevalence</a> of alcohol and illicit drug addictions were estimated at 12% and 2–3% respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Epidem_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-Epidem-166">[154]</a></sup> The lifetime prevalence of <a href="/wiki/Prescription_drug" title="Prescription drug">prescription drug</a> addictions is currently around 4.7%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2016,<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> about 22 million people in the United States need treatment for an addiction to alcohol, nicotine, or other drugs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABAM_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-ABAM-168">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIDA_Addiction_Medicine_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIDA_Addiction_Medicine-169">[157]</a></sup> Only about 10%, or a little over 2 million, receive any form of treatments, and those that do generally do not receive <a href="/wiki/Evidence-based_medicine" title="Evidence-based medicine">evidence-based care</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABAM_168-1"><a href="#cite_note-ABAM-168">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIDA_Addiction_Medicine_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-NIDA_Addiction_Medicine-169">[157]</a></sup> One-third of <a href="/wiki/Inpatient_care" title="Inpatient care">inpatient</a> hospital costs and 20% of all deaths in the US every year are the result of untreated addictions and risky substance use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABAM_168-2"><a href="#cite_note-ABAM-168">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIDA_Addiction_Medicine_169-2"><a href="#cite_note-NIDA_Addiction_Medicine-169">[157]</a></sup> In spite of the massive overall economic cost to society, which is greater than the cost of <a href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a> and all forms of <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a> combined, most doctors in the US lack the training to effectively address a drug addiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABAM_168-3"><a href="#cite_note-ABAM-168">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIDA_Addiction_Medicine_169-3"><a href="#cite_note-NIDA_Addiction_Medicine-169">[157]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another review listed estimates of lifetime prevalence rates for several behavioral addictions in the United States, including 1–2% for compulsive gambling, 5% for sexual addiction, 2.8% for food addiction, and 5–6% for compulsive shopping.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-37"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16">[15]</a></sup> A systematic review indicated that the time-invariant prevalence rate for sexual addiction and related compulsive sexual behavior (e.g., compulsive masturbation with or without pornography, compulsive cybersex, etc.) within the United States ranges from 3–6% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Systematic_review_-_yet_another_DSM_fail_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-Systematic_review_-_yet_another_DSM_fail-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to a 2017 poll conducted by the <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a>, almost half of US adults know a family member or close friend who has struggled with a drug addiction at some point in their life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2019, opioid addiction was acknowledged as a national crisis in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[159]</a></sup> An article in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> stated that "America's largest drug companies flooded the country with pain pills from 2006 through 2012, even when it became apparent that they were fueling addiction and overdoses."
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_America">South America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: South America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The realities of opioid use and <a href="/wiki/Opioid_use_disorder" title="Opioid use disorder">opioid use disorder</a> in Latin America may be deceptive if observations are limited to epidemiological findings. In the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Office_on_Drugs_and_Crime" title="United Nations Office on Drugs and Crime">United Nations Office on Drugs and Crime</a> report,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[160]</a></sup> although South America produced 3% of the world's morphine and heroin and 0.01% of its opium, prevalence of use is uneven. According to the Inter-American Commission on Drug Abuse Control, consumption of heroin is low in most Latin American countries, although Colombia is the area's largest opium producer. Mexico, because of its border with the United States, has the highest incidence of use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[161]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_and_etymology">History and etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: History and etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Recreational_drug_use" title="Recreational drug use">Recreational drug use</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_models_of_human_drug_use" title="Evolutionary models of human drug use">Evolutionary models of human drug use</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_drinking" title="History of drinking">History of drinking</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_smoking" title="History of smoking">History of smoking</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Substance_abuse_in_Ancient_Rome" title="Substance abuse in Ancient Rome">Substance abuse in Ancient Rome</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Etymology" title="Etymology">etymology</a> of the term <i>addiction</i> throughout history has been often misunderstood and has taken on various meanings associated with the word.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crocq_2007_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crocq_2007-174">[162]</a></sup> An example is the usage of the word in the religious landscape of <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_Europe" title="Early modern Europe">early modern Europe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lemon_2018_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lemon_2018-175">[163]</a></sup> "Addiction" at the time meant "to attach" to something, giving it both positive and negative connotations. The object of this attachment could be characterised as "good or bad".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosenthal_2019_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rosenthal_2019-176">[164]</a></sup> However, the meaning of addiction during the <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">early modern period</a> was mostly associated with positivity and goodness;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lemon_2018_175-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lemon_2018-175">[163]</a></sup> during this early modern and highly religious era of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_revivalism" title="Christian revivalism">Christian revivalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pietism" title="Pietism">Pietistic tendencies</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lemon_2018_175-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lemon_2018-175">[163]</a></sup> it was seen as a way of "devoting oneself to another".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosenthal_2019_176-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rosenthal_2019-176">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Addiction_medicine" title="Addiction medicine">Modern research on addiction</a> has led to a better understanding of the disease with research studies on the topic dating back to 1875, specifically on <a href="/wiki/Morphine" title="Morphine">morphine addiction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[165]</a></sup> This furthered the understanding of addiction being a medical condition. It wasn't until the 19th century that addiction was seen and acknowledged in the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a> as a disease, being both a physical condition and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mental_illness" title="Mental illness">mental illness</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[166]</a></sup> Today, addiction is understood both as a <a href="/wiki/Biopsychosocial_model" title="Biopsychosocial model">biopsychosocial</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brain_disorder" title="Brain disorder">neurological disorder</a> that negatively impacts those who are affected by it, most commonly associated with the use of <a href="/wiki/Substance_use_disorder" title="Substance use disorder">drugs</a> and excessive use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcohol_use_disorder" title="Alcohol use disorder">alcohol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIDA2020_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-NIDA2020-8">[7]</a></sup> The understanding of addiction has changed throughout history, which has impacted and continues to impact the ways it is medically treated and diagnosed.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Society and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:An_alcoholic_man_with_delirium_Wellcome_L0060780_(level_correction).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6056" data-file-width="4231" decoding="async" height="315" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/An_alcoholic_man_with_delirium_Wellcome_L0060780_%28level_correction%29.jpg/220px-An_alcoholic_man_with_delirium_Wellcome_L0060780_%28level_correction%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/An_alcoholic_man_with_delirium_Wellcome_L0060780_%28level_correction%29.jpg/330px-An_alcoholic_man_with_delirium_Wellcome_L0060780_%28level_correction%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/An_alcoholic_man_with_delirium_Wellcome_L0060780_%28level_correction%29.jpg/440px-An_alcoholic_man_with_delirium_Wellcome_L0060780_%28level_correction%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:An_alcoholic_man_with_delirium_Wellcome_L0060780_(level_correction).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Acute confusional state caused by <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_withdrawal_syndrome" title="Alcohol withdrawal syndrome">alcohol withdrawal</a>, also known as <a href="/wiki/Delirium_tremens" title="Delirium tremens">delirium tremens</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span id="A_biopsychosocial.E2.80.93spiritual_understanding_of_addiction"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="A_biopsychosocial–spiritual_understanding_of_addiction">A biopsychosocial–spiritual understanding of addiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: A biopsychosocial–spiritual understanding of addiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While regarded <a href="/wiki/Biomedicine" title="Biomedicine">biomedically</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Neuropsychology" title="Neuropsychology">neuropsychological</a> disorder, addiction is multi-layered, with <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biological</a>, <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychological</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social" title="Social">social</a>, <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">cultural</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spiritual</a> (biopsychosocial–spiritual) elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[168]</a></sup> A biopsychosocial–spiritual approach fosters the crossing of <a href="/wiki/Academic_discipline" title="Academic discipline">disciplinary</a> boundaries, and promotes <a href="/wiki/Holism" title="Holism">holistic</a> considerations of addiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[171]</a></sup> A biopsychosocial–spiritual approach considers, for example, how physical environments influence experiences, habits, and patterns of addiction.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ethnography" title="Ethnography">Ethnographic</a> engagements and developments in fields of knowledge have contributed to biopsychosocial–spiritual understandings of addiction, including the work of <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Bourgois" title="Philippe Bourgois">Philippe Bourgois</a>, whose <a href="/wiki/Field_research#In_anthropology" title="Field research">fieldwork</a> with street-level drug dealers in <a href="/wiki/East_Harlem" title="East Harlem">East Harlem</a> highlights correlations between drug use and structural <a href="/wiki/Oppression" title="Oppression">oppression</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[172]</a></sup><a href="/wiki/Addiction#_ftn3" title="Addiction"><sup>[3]</sup></a> Prior models that have informed the prevailing biopsychosocial–spiritual consideration of addiction include:
</p>
<h4><span id="The_cultural_model_.281950s.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_cultural_model_(1950s)">The cultural model (1950s)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: The cultural model (1950s)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The cultural model, an <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropological</a> understanding of the emergence of drug use and <a href="/wiki/Substance_abuse" title="Substance abuse">abuse</a>, was developed by <a href="/wiki/Dwight_B._Heath" title="Dwight B. Heath">Dwight Heath</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-185">[173]</a></sup> Heath undertook <a href="/wiki/Ethnography" title="Ethnography">ethnographic</a> research and <a href="/wiki/Field_research" title="Field research">fieldwork</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Camba" title="Camba">Camba</a> people of <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a> from June 1956 to August 1957.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-186">[174]</a></sup> Heath observed that adult members of society drank ‘large quantities of <a href="/wiki/Rum" title="Rum">rum</a> and became intoxicated for several contiguous days at least twice a month’.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-187">[175]</a></sup> This frequent, heavy drinking from which <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_intoxication" title="Alcohol intoxication">intoxication</a> followed was typically undertaken socially, during <a href="/wiki/Festival" title="Festival">festivals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[176]</a></sup> Having returned in 1989, Heath observed that whilst much had changed, ‘drinking parties’ remained, as per his initial observations, and ‘there appear to be no harmful consequences to anyone’.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[177]</a></sup> Heath’s observations and <a href="/wiki/Interaction" title="Interaction">interactions</a> reflected that this form of <a href="/wiki/Social_behavior" title="Social behavior">social behaviour</a>, the habitual heavy consumption of alcohol, was encouraged and valued, enforcing social bonds in the Camba community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_186-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-186">[174]</a></sup> Despite frequent <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_intoxication" title="Alcohol intoxication">intoxication</a>, “even to the point of unconsciousness”, the Camba held no concept of <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism" title="Alcoholism">alcoholism</a> (a form of addiction), and no visible social problems associated with drunkenness, or addiction, were apparent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_185-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-185">[173]</a></sup>
</p><p>As noted by <a href="/wiki/Merrill_Singer" title="Merrill Singer">Merrill Singer</a>, Heath’s findings, when considered alongside subsequent cross-cultural experiences, challenged the perception that <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Intoxication" title="Intoxication">intoxication</a> is socially ‘inherently disruptive’.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_185-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-185">[173]</a></sup> Following this <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fieldwork" title="Fieldwork">fieldwork</a>, Heath proposed the ‘cultural model’, suggesting that ‘problems’ associated with heavy drinking, such as alcoholism – a recognised form addiction – were <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">cultural</a>: that is, that <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism" title="Alcoholism">alcoholism</a> is determined by cultural beliefs, and therefore varies among cultures. Heath’s findings challenged the notion that ‘continued use [of alcohol] is inexorably addictive and damaging to the consumer’s health’.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_186-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-186">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_185-3"><a href="#cite_note-:2-185">[173]</a></sup>
</p><p>The cultural model did face criticism by <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">Sociologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Robin_Room" title="Robin Room">Robin Room</a> and others, who felt anthropologists could “downgrade the severity of the problem”.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-187">[175]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Merrill_Singer" title="Merrill Singer">Merrill Singer</a> found it notable that the ethnographers working within the prominence of the cultural model were part of the ‘wet generation’: while not blind to the ‘disruptive, dysfunctional and debilitating effects of alcohol consumption’, they were products ‘socialized to view alcohol consumption as normal’.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_187-2"><a href="#cite_note-:5-187">[175]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Subcultural_Model">The Subcultural Model</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: The Subcultural Model">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Historically, addiction has been viewed from the <a href="/wiki/Emic_and_etic" title="Emic and etic">etic</a> perspective, defining users through the <a href="/wiki/Pathology" title="Pathology">pathology</a> of their condition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[178]</a></sup> As reports of drug use rapidly increased, the cultural model found application in anthropological <a href="/wiki/Research" title="Research">research</a> exploring <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">western</a> drug <a href="/wiki/Subculture" title="Subculture">subculture</a> practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_187-3"><a href="#cite_note-:5-187">[175]</a></sup>
</p><p>The approach evolved from the ethnographic exploration into the lived experiences and <a href="/wiki/Subjectivity" title="Subjectivity">subjectivities</a> of <a href="/wiki/1960s" title="1960s">1960s</a> and <a href="/wiki/1970s" title="1970s">1970s</a> drug subcultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_187-4"><a href="#cite_note-:5-187">[175]</a></sup> The seminal publication “Taking care of business”, by Edward Preble and John J. Casey, documented the daily lives of <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a> street-based intravenous <a href="/wiki/Heroin" title="Heroin">heroin</a> users in rich detail, providing unique insight into the dynamic social worlds and activities that surrounded their drug use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[179]</a></sup>
</p><p>These findings challenge popular narratives of <a href="/wiki/Immorality" title="Immorality">immorality</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deviance_(sociology)" title="Deviance (sociology)">deviance</a>, conceptualising substance abuse as a social <a href="/wiki/Phenomenon" title="Phenomenon">phenomenon</a>. Furthermore, it suggests that the prevailing culture can have a greater influence on drug taking behaviours than the physical and <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychological</a> effects of the drug itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-192">[180]</a></sup> To <a href="/wiki/Social_exclusion" title="Social exclusion">marginalised</a> individuals, drug subcultures can provide social connection, <a href="/wiki/Symbol" title="Symbol">symbolic</a> meaning, and socially constructed purpose that they may feel is unattainable through conventional means.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-192">[180]</a></sup> The subcultural model demonstrates the complexities of addiction, highlighting the need for an integrated approach. It contends that a <a href="/wiki/Biosocial_theory" title="Biosocial theory">biosocial</a> approach is required to achieve a <a href="/wiki/Holism" title="Holism">holistic</a> understanding of addiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_187-5"><a href="#cite_note-:5-187">[175]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Critical_Medical_Anthropology_Model">The Critical Medical Anthropology Model</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: The Critical Medical Anthropology Model">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Emerging in the early <a href="/wiki/1980s" title="1980s">1980s</a>, the critical medical anthropology model was introduced, and as <a href="/wiki/Merrill_Singer" title="Merrill Singer">Merrill Singer</a> offers ‘was applied quickly to the analysis of drug use’.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_187-6"><a href="#cite_note-:5-187">[175]</a></sup> Where the cultural model of the <a href="/wiki/1950s" title="1950s">1950s</a> looked at the social body, the critical medical anthropology model revealed the <a href="/wiki/Body_politic" title="Body politic">body politic</a>, considering drug use and addiction within the context of macro level structures including larger political systems, <a href="/wiki/Economic_inequality" title="Economic inequality">economic inequalities</a>, and the institutional power held over social processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_187-7"><a href="#cite_note-:5-187">[175]</a></sup>
</p><p>Highly relevant to addiction, the three issues emphasized in the model are:
</p>
<ol><li>The social production of suffering</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Self-medication" title="Self-medication">Self-medication</a></li>
<li>The political economy (<a href="/wiki/Licit_and_Illicit_Drugs" title="Licit and Illicit Drugs">licit and illicit drugs</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_185-4"><a href="#cite_note-:2-185">[173]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>These three key points highlight how drugs may come to be used to self-medicate the <a href="/wiki/Psychological_trauma" title="Psychological trauma">psychological trauma</a> of socio-political disparity and <a href="/wiki/Injustice" title="Injustice">injustice</a>, intertwining with <a href="/wiki/Licit_and_Illicit_Drugs" title="Licit and Illicit Drugs">licit and illicit drug</a> market politics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_187-8"><a href="#cite_note-:5-187">[175]</a></sup> Social suffering, “the misery among those on the weaker end of power relations in terms of physical health, <a href="/wiki/Mental_health" title="Mental health">mental health</a> and lived experience”, is used by anthropologists to analyse how individuals may have personal problems caused by political and economic power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_187-9"><a href="#cite_note-:5-187">[175]</a></sup> From the perspective of critical medical anthropology heavy drug use and addiction is a consequence of such larger scale unequal distributions of power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_187-10"><a href="#cite_note-:5-187">[175]</a></sup>
</p><p>The three models developed here – the cultural model, the subcultural model, and the Critical Medical Anthropology Model – display how addiction is not an experience to be considered only biomedically. Through consideration of addiction alongside the <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biological</a>, <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychological</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social" title="Social">social</a>, <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">cultural</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spiritual</a> (biopsychosocial–spiritual) elements which influence its experience, a holistic and comprehensive understanding can be built.
</p><p>Addiction causes an "astoundingly high financial and human toll" on individuals and society as a whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Societal_toll_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-Societal_toll-193">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Epidem_166-2"><a href="#cite_note-Epidem-166">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABAM_168-4"><a href="#cite_note-ABAM-168">[156]</a></sup> In the United States, the total economic cost to society is greater than that of all types of diabetes and all cancers combined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABAM_168-5"><a href="#cite_note-ABAM-168">[156]</a></sup> These costs arise from the direct adverse effects of drugs and associated healthcare costs (e.g., emergency medical services and outpatient and inpatient care), long-term complications (e.g., lung cancer from smoking tobacco products, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liver_cirrhosis" title="Liver cirrhosis">liver cirrhosis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alcohol-related_dementia" title="Alcohol-related dementia">dementia</a> from chronic alcohol consumption, and <a href="/wiki/Meth_mouth" title="Meth mouth">meth mouth</a> from methamphetamine use), the loss of productivity and associated welfare costs, fatal and non-fatal accidents (e.g., traffic collisions), suicides, homicides, and incarceration, among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Societal_toll_193-1"><a href="#cite_note-Societal_toll-193">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Epidem_166-3"><a href="#cite_note-Epidem-166">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABAM_168-6"><a href="#cite_note-ABAM-168">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-INCB_2013_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-INCB_2013-194">[182]</a></sup> In the United States, a study conducted by the National Institute on Drug Abuse has found that overdose deaths in the United States have almost tripled amongst male and females from 2002 to 2017, with 72,306 overdose deaths reported in 2017 in the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[183]</a></sup> 2020 marked the year with highest number of overdose deaths over a 12-month period, with 81,000 overdose deaths, exceeding the records set in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[184]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="The_suffixes_.22-holic.22_and_.22-holism.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='The_suffixes_"-holic"_and_"-holism"'>The suffixes "-holic" and "-holism"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title='Edit section: The suffixes "-holic" and "-holism"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In contemporary modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_(language)" title="English (language)">English</a> "-holic" is a <a href="/wiki/Suffix" title="Suffix">suffix</a> that can be added to a subject to denote an addiction to it. It was extracted from the word <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism" title="Alcoholism">alcoholism</a> (one of the first addictions to be widely identified both medically and socially) (correctly the root "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/alcohol" title="wikt:alcohol">wikt:alcohol</a>" plus the suffix "-ism") by misdividing or <a href="/wiki/Rebracketing" title="Rebracketing">rebracketing</a> it into "alco" and "-holism". (Another such misdivision is interpreting "<a href="/wiki/Helicopter" title="Helicopter">helicopter</a>" as "heli-copter" rather than the etymologically correct "helico-pter", giving rise to such derived words as "<a href="/wiki/Heliport" title="Heliport">heliport</a>" and "jetcopter".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[185]</a></sup>) There are correct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medico-legal" title="Medico-legal">medico-legal</a> terms for such addictions: <a href="/wiki/Dipsomania" title="Dipsomania">dipsomania</a> is the medico-legal term for <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism" title="Alcoholism">alcoholism</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[186]</a></sup> other examples are in this table:
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
