<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Endnotes">Endnotes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Endnotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In other words, a person cannot control the neurobiological processes that occur in the body in response to using an addictive drug. A person can make a voluntary choice to, for example, start using a drug or to seek help after becoming addicted, although resisting the urge to use becomes increasingly difficult as addiction worsens. See <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heilig-et-al-2021_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Heilig-et-al-2021-6">[6]</a></sup> for detailed discussion.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1011085734" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-60">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to a review of experimental animal models that examined the transgenerational epigenetic inheritance of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetic_mark&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Epigenetic mark (page does not exist)">epigenetic marks</a> that occur in addiction, alterations in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Histone_acetylation" title="Histone acetylation">histone acetylation</a> – specifically, di-acetylation of <a href="/wiki/Lysine" title="Lysine">lysine</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Residue_(amino_acid)" title="Residue (amino acid)">residues</a> 9 and 14 on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Histone_3" title="Histone 3">histone 3</a> (i.e., <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=H3K9ac2&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="H3K9ac2 (page does not exist)">H3K9ac2</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=H3K14ac2&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="H3K14ac2 (page does not exist)">H3K14ac2</a>) in association with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BDNF" title="BDNF">BDNF</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gene_promoter" title="Gene promoter">gene promoters</a> – have been shown to occur within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medial_prefrontal_cortex" title="Medial prefrontal cortex">medial prefrontal cortex</a> (mPFC), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Testes" title="Testes">testes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sperm" title="Sperm">sperm</a> of cocaine-addicted male rats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction_33-8"><a href="#cite_note-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction-33">[32]</a></sup> These epigenetic alterations in the rat mPFC result in increased BDNF <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">gene expression</a> within the mPFC, which in turn blunts the <a href="/wiki/Reward_system" title="Reward system">rewarding properties</a> of cocaine and reduces cocaine <a href="/wiki/Self-administration" title="Self-administration">self-administration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction_33-9"><a href="#cite_note-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction-33">[32]</a></sup> The male but not female offspring of these cocaine-exposed rats inherited both epigenetic marks (i.e., di-acetylation of lysine residues 9 and 14 on histone 3) within mPFC neurons, the corresponding increase in BDNF expression within mPFC neurons, and the behavioral phenotype associated with these effects (i.e., a reduction in cocaine reward, resulting in reduced cocaine-seeking by these male offspring).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction_33-10"><a href="#cite_note-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction-33">[32]</a></sup> Consequently, the transmission of these two cocaine-induced epigenetic alterations (i.e., H3K9ac2 and H3K14ac2) in rats from male fathers to male offspring served to reduce the offspring's risk of developing an addiction to cocaine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction_33-11"><a href="#cite_note-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction-33">[32]</a></sup> As of 2018,<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Addiction&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> neither the heritability of these epigenetic marks in humans nor the behavioral effects of the marks within human mPFC neurons has been established.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction_33-12"><a href="#cite_note-Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance_in_addiction-33">[32]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-ΔFosB_behaviors-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-ΔFosB_behaviors_63-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ΔFosB_behaviors_63-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">A decrease in aversion sensitivity, in simpler terms, means that an individual's behavior is less likely to be influenced by undesirable outcomes.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-94"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-94">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In other words, c-Fos <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gene_repression" title="Gene repression">repression</a> allows ΔFosB to more rapidly accumulate within the D1-type medium spiny neurons of the nucleus accumbens because it is selectively induced in this state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-16"><a href="#cite_note-Cellular_basis-3">[3]</a></sup> Prior to c-Fos repression, all Fos family proteins (e.g., c-Fos, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fra1" title="Fra1">Fra1</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fra2" title="Fra2">Fra2</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FosB" title="FosB">FosB</a>, and ΔFosB) are induced together, with ΔFosB expression increasing to a lesser extent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-17"><a href="#cite_note-Cellular_basis-3">[3]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-95"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-95">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to two medical reviews, ΔFosB has been implicated in causing both increases and decreases in dynorphin expression in different studies;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-What_the_ΔFosB?_61-8"><a href="#cite_note-What_the_ΔFosB?-61">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18640924_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18640924-93">[89]</a></sup> this table entry reflects only a decrease. </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-98"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-98">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Incentive salience, the "<a href="/wiki/Motivational_salience" title="Motivational salience">motivational salience</a>" for a reward, is a "desire" or "want" attribute, which includes a motivational component, that the brain assigns to a rewarding stimulus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Incentive_salience_and_motivation_review_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Incentive_salience_and_motivation_review-96">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAcc_function_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAcc_function-97">[91]</a></sup> As a consequence, incentive salience acts as a motivational "magnet" for a rewarding stimulus that commands attention, induces approach, and causes the rewarding stimulus to be sought out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Incentive_salience_and_motivation_review_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-Incentive_salience_and_motivation_review-96">[90]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-101"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-101">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In simplest terms, this means that when either amphetamine or sex is perceived as more alluring or desirable through reward sensitization, this effect occurs with the other as well.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-150"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-150">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Inhibitors of class I <a href="/wiki/Histone_deacetylase" title="Histone deacetylase">histone deacetylase</a> (HDAC) enzymes are drugs that inhibit four specific <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Histone-modifying_enzyme" title="Histone-modifying enzyme">histone-modifying enzymes</a>: <a href="/wiki/HDAC1" title="HDAC1">HDAC1</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HDAC2" title="HDAC2">HDAC2</a>, <a href="/wiki/HDAC3" title="HDAC3">HDAC3</a>, and <a href="/wiki/HDAC8" title="HDAC8">HDAC8</a>. Most of the animal research with HDAC inhibitors has been conducted with four drugs: <a href="/wiki/Butyric_acid" title="Butyric acid">butyrate salts</a> (mainly <a href="/wiki/Sodium_butyrate" title="Sodium butyrate">sodium butyrate</a>), <a href="/wiki/Trichostatin_A" title="Trichostatin A">trichostatin A</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valproic_acid" title="Valproic acid">valproic acid</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vorinostat" title="Vorinostat">SAHA</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amphetamine_epigenetics_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Amphetamine_epigenetics-149">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chromatin_states_102-3"><a href="#cite_note-Chromatin_states-102">[94]</a></sup> butyric acid is a naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Short-chain_fatty_acid" title="Short-chain fatty acid">short-chain fatty acid</a> in humans, while the latter two compounds are FDA-approved drugs with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_indication" title="Medical indication">medical indications</a> unrelated to addiction.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-152"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-152">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Specifically, prolonged administration of a class I HDAC inhibitor appears to reduce an animal's motivation to acquire and use an addictive drug without affecting an animals motivation to attain other rewards (i.e., it does not appear to cause <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Motivational_anhedonia" title="Motivational anhedonia">motivational anhedonia</a>) and reduce the amount of the drug that is <a href="/wiki/Self-administration" title="Self-administration">self-administered</a> when it is readily available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nestler_2014_epigenetics_76-6"><a href="#cite_note-Nestler_2014_epigenetics-76">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chromatin_states_102-5"><a href="#cite_note-Chromatin_states-102">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HDACi_primaries_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-HDACi_primaries-151">[142]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-155"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-155">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Among the few clinical trials that employed a class I HDAC inhibitor, one utilized <a href="/wiki/Valproate" title="Valproate">valproate</a> for methamphetamine addiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Valproate_for_methamphetamine_addiction_primary_source_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Valproate_for_methamphetamine_addiction_primary_source-154">[144]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-165"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-165">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The lifetime prevalence of an addiction is the percentage of individuals in a population that developed an addiction at some point in their life.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-Brain_disease-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Brain_disease_2-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Brain_disease_2-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Brain_disease_2-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFVolkowKoobMcLellan2016">Volkow ND, Koob GF, McLellan AT (January 2016). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC6135257" rel="nofollow">"Neurobiologic Advances from the Brain Disease Model of Addiction"</a>. <i>New England Journal of Medicine</i>. <b>374</b> (4): 363–371. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1056%2FNEJMra1511480" rel="nofollow">10.1056/NEJMra1511480</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC6135257" rel="nofollow">6135257</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26816013" rel="nofollow">26816013</a>. <q>Substance-use disorder: A diagnostic term in the fifth edition of the Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders (DSM-5) referring to recurrent use of alcohol or other drugs that causes clinically and functionally significant impairment, such as health problems, disability, and failure to meet major responsibilities at work, school, or home. Depending on the level of severity, this disorder is classified as mild, moderate, or severe.<br/>Addiction: A term used to indicate the most severe, chronic stage of substance-use disorder, in which there is a substantial loss of self-control, as indicated by compulsive drug taking despite the desire to stop taking the drug. In the DSM-5, the term addiction is synonymous with the classification of severe substance-use disorder.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=New+England+Journal+of+Medicine&amp;rft.atitle=Neurobiologic+Advances+from+the+Brain+Disease+Model+of+Addiction&amp;rft.volume=374&amp;rft.issue=4&amp;rft.pages=363-371&amp;rft.date=2016-01&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC6135257%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F26816013&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1056%2FNEJMra1511480&amp;rft.aulast=Volkow&amp;rft.aufirst=ND&amp;rft.au=Koob%2C+GF&amp;rft.au=McLellan%2C+AT&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC6135257&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAddiction"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Cellular_basis-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-7"><sup><i><b>h</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-8"><sup><i><b>i</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-9"><sup><i><b>j</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-10"><sup><i><b>k</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-11"><sup><i><b>l</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-12"><sup><i><b>m</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-13"><sup><i><b>n</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-14"><sup><i><b>o</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-15"><sup><i><b>p</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-16"><sup><i><b>q</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-17"><sup><i><b>r</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-18"><sup><i><b>s</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Cellular_basis_3-19"><sup><i><b>t</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFNestler_EJ2013">Nestler EJ (December 2013). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3898681" rel="nofollow">"Cellular basis of memory for addiction"</a>. <i>Dialogues in Clinical Neuroscience</i>. <b>15</b> (4): 431–443. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3898681" rel="nofollow">3898681</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/24459410" rel="nofollow">24459410</a>. <q>Despite the importance of numerous psychosocial factors, at its core, drug addiction involves a biological process: the ability of repeated exposure to a drug of abuse to induce changes in a vulnerable brain that drive the compulsive seeking and taking of drugs, and loss of control over drug use, that define a state of addiction. ... A large body of literature has demonstrated that such ΔFosB induction in D1-type [nucleus accumbens] neurons increases an animal's sensitivity to drug as well as natural rewards and promotes drug self-administration, presumably through a process of positive reinforcement ... Another ΔFosB target is cFos: as ΔFosB accumulates with repeated drug exposure it represses c-Fos and contributes to the molecular switch whereby ΔFosB is selectively induced in the chronic drug-treated state.<sup>41</sup> ... Moreover, there is increasing evidence that, despite a range of genetic risks for addiction across the population, exposure to sufficiently high doses of a drug for long periods of time can transform someone who has relatively lower genetic loading into an addict.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Dialogues+in+Clinical+Neuroscience&amp;rft.atitle=Cellular+basis+of+memory+for+addiction&amp;rft.volume=15&amp;rft.issue=4&amp;rft.pages=431-443&amp;rft.date=2013-12&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3898681%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F24459410&amp;rft.au=Nestler+EJ&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3898681&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAddiction"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Addiction_glossary-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Addiction_glossary_4-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFMalenkaNestlerHyman2009">Malenka RC, Nestler EJ, Hyman SE (2009). "Chapter 15: Reinforcement and Addictive Disorders".  In Sydor A, Brown RY (eds.). <i>Molecular Neuropharmacology: A Foundation for Clinical Neuroscience</i> (2nd ed.). New York: McGraw-Hill Medical. pp. 364–375. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780071481274" title="Special:BookSources/9780071481274"><bdi>9780071481274</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Chapter+15%3A+Reinforcement+and+Addictive+Disorders&amp;rft.btitle=Molecular+Neuropharmacology%3A+A+Foundation+for+Clinical+Neuroscience&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pages=364-375&amp;rft.edition=2nd&amp;rft.pub=McGraw-Hill+Medical&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft.isbn=9780071481274&amp;rft.aulast=Malenka&amp;rft.aufirst=RC&amp;rft.au=Nestler%2C+EJ&amp;rft.au=Hyman%2C+SE&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAddiction"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Nestler_Labs_Glossary-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Nestler_Labs_Glossary_5-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="http://neuroscience.mssm.edu/nestler/glossary.html" rel="nofollow">"Glossary of Terms"</a>. <i>Mount Sinai School of Medicine</i>. Department of Neuroscience<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">9 February</span> 2015</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Mount+Sinai+School+of+Medicine&amp;rft.atitle=Glossary+of+Terms&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fneuroscience.mssm.edu%2Fnestler%2Fglossary.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAddiction"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Heilig-et-al-2021-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Heilig-et-al-2021_6-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Heilig-et-al-2021_6-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFHeiligMacKillopMartinezRehm2021">Heilig M, MacKillop J, Martinez D, Rehm J, Leggio L, Vanderschuren LJ (September 2021). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC8357831" rel="nofollow">"Addiction as a brain disease revised: why it still matters, and the need for consilience"</a>. <i>Neuropsychopharmacology</i>. <b>46</b> (10): 1715–1723. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fs41386-020-00950-y" rel="nofollow">10.1038/s41386-020-00950-y</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC8357831" rel="nofollow">8357831</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/33619327" rel="nofollow">33619327</a>. <q>pre-existing vulnerabilities and persistent drug use lead to a vicious circle of substantive disruptions in the brain that impair and undermine choice capacities for adaptive behavior, but do not annihilate them.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Neuropsychopharmacology&amp;rft.atitle=Addiction+as+a+brain+disease+revised%3A+why+it+still+matters%2C+and+the+need+for+consilience&amp;rft.volume=46&amp;rft.issue=10&amp;rft.pages=1715-1723&amp;rft.date=2021-09&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC8357831%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F33619327&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1038%2Fs41386-020-00950-y&amp;rft.aulast=Heilig&amp;rft.aufirst=M&amp;rft.au=MacKillop%2C+J&amp;rft.au=Martinez%2C+D&amp;rft.au=Rehm%2C+J&amp;rft.au=Leggio%2C+L&amp;rft.au=Vanderschuren%2C+LJ&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC8357831&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAddiction"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-NHMH_3e_terms-DSM_flaw-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-NHMH_3e_terms-DSM_flaw_15-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-NHMH_3e_terms-DSM_flaw_15-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFMalenkaNestlerHymanHoltzman2015">Malenka RC, Nestler EJ, Hyman SE, Holtzman DM (2015). "Chapter 16: Reinforcement and Addictive Disorders". <i>Molecular Neuropharmacology: A Foundation for Clinical Neuroscience</i> (3rd ed.). New York: McGraw-Hill Medical. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-07-182770-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-07-182770-6"><bdi>978-0-07-182770-6</bdi></a>. <q>The official diagnosis of drug addiction by the Diagnostic and Statistic Manual of Mental Disorders (2013), which uses the term substance use disorder, is flawed. Criteria used to make the diagnosis of substance use disorders include tolerance and somatic dependence/withdrawal, even though these processes are not integral to addiction as noted. It is ironic and unfortunate that the manual still avoids use of the term addiction as an official diagnosis, even though addiction provides the best description of the clinical syndrome.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Chapter+16%3A+Reinforcement+and+Addictive+Disorders&amp;rft.btitle=Molecular+Neuropharmacology%3A+A+Foundation+for+Clinical+Neuroscience&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.edition=3rd&amp;rft.pub=McGraw-Hill+Medical&amp;rft.date=2015&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-07-182770-6&amp;rft.aulast=Malenka&amp;rft.aufirst=RC&amp;rft.au=Nestler%2C+EJ&amp;rft.au=Hyman%2C+SE&amp;rft.au=Holtzman%2C+DM&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAddiction"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Natural_and_drug_addictions-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-21"><sup><i><b>v</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-22"><sup><i><b>w</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-25"><sup><i><b>z</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-26"><sup><i><b>aa</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-27"><sup><i><b>ab</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-28"><sup><i><b>ac</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-29"><sup><i><b>ad</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-30"><sup><i><b>ae</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-31"><sup><i><b>af</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-32"><sup><i><b>ag</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-33"><sup><i><b>ah</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-34"><sup><i><b>ai</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-35"><sup><i><b>aj</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-36"><sup><i><b>ak</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Natural_and_drug_addictions_16-37"><sup><i><b>al</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFOlsen2011">Olsen CM (December 2011). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3139704" rel="nofollow">"Natural rewards, neuroplasticity, and non-drug addictions"</a>. <i>Neuropharmacology</i>. <b>61</b> (7): 1109–22. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.neuropharm.2011.03.010" rel="nofollow">10.1016/j.neuropharm.2011.03.010</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3139704" rel="nofollow">3139704</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/21459101" rel="nofollow">21459101</a>. <q>Functional neuroimaging studies in humans have shown that gambling (Breiter et al, 2001), shopping (Knutson et al, 2007), orgasm (<a href="/wiki/Barry_Komisaruk" title="Barry Komisaruk">Komisaruk</a> et al, 2004), playing video games (Koepp et al, 1998; Hoeft et al, 2008) and the sight of appetizing food (Wang et al, 2004a) activate many of the same brain regions (i.e., the mesocorticolimbic system and extended amygdala) as drugs of abuse (Volkow et al, 2004). ... Cross-sensitization is also bidirectional, as a history of amphetamine administration facilitates sexual behavior and enhances the associated increase in NAc DA ... As described for food reward, sexual experience can also lead to activation of plasticity-related signaling cascades. The transcription factor delta FosB is increased in the NAc, PFC, dorsal striatum, and VTA following repeated sexual behavior (Wallace et al., 2008; Pitchers et al., 2010b). This natural increase in delta FosB or viral overexpression of delta FosB within the NAc modulates sexual performance, and NAc blockade of delta FosB attenuates this behavior (Hedges et al, 2009; Pitchers et al., 2010b). Further, viral overexpression of delta FosB enhances the conditioned place preference for an environment paired with sexual experience (Hedges et al., 2009). ... In some people, there is a transition from "normal" to compulsive engagement in natural rewards (such as food or sex), a condition that some have termed behavioral or non-drug addictions (Holden, 2001; Grant et al., 2006a). ... In humans, the role of dopamine signaling in incentive-sensitization processes has recently been highlighted by the observation of a dopamine dysregulation syndrome in some patients taking dopaminergic drugs. This syndrome is characterized by a medication-induced increase in (or compulsive) engagement in non-drug rewards such as gambling, shopping, or sex (Evans et al, 2006; Aiken, 2007; Lader, 2008)."</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Neuropharmacology&amp;rft.atitle=Natural+rewards%2C+neuroplasticity%2C+and+non-drug+addictions&amp;rft.volume=61&amp;rft.issue=7&amp;rft.pages=1109-22&amp;rft.date=2011-12&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3139704%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F21459101&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.neuropharm.2011.03.010&amp;rft.aulast=Olsen&amp;rft.aufirst=CM&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3139704&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAddiction"></span><br/><a class="external text" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3139704/table/T1/" rel="nofollow">Table 1: Summary of plasticity observed following exposure to drug or natural reinforcers</a>"</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Nestler-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Nestler_17-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Nestler_17-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Nestler_17-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Nestler_17-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Nestler_17-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Nestler_17-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Nestler_17-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Nestler_17-7"><sup><i><b>h</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Nestler_17-8"><sup><i><b>i</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Nestler_17-9"><sup><i><b>j</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Nestler_17-10"><sup><i><b>k</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Nestler_17-11"><sup><i><b>l</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Nestler_17-12"><sup><i><b>m</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFRobisonNestler2011">Robison AJ, Nestler EJ (November 2011). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3272277" rel="nofollow">"Transcriptional and epigenetic mechanisms of addiction"</a>. <i>Nat. Rev. Neurosci</i>. <b>12</b> (11): 623–37. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fnrn3111" rel="nofollow">10.1038/nrn3111</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3272277" rel="nofollow">3272277</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/21989194" rel="nofollow">21989194</a>. <q>ΔFosB has been linked directly to several addiction-related behaviors ... Importantly, genetic or viral overexpression of ΔJunD, a dominant negative mutant of JunD which antagonizes ΔFosB- and other AP-1-mediated transcriptional activity, in the NAc or OFC blocks these key effects of drug exposure<sup>14,22–24</sup>. This indicates that ΔFosB is both necessary and sufficient for many of the changes wrought in the brain by chronic drug exposure. ΔFosB is also induced in D1-type NAc MSNs by chronic consumption of several natural rewards, including sucrose, high fat food, sex, wheel running, where it promotes that consumption<sup>14,26–30</sup>. This implicates ΔFosB in the regulation of natural rewards under normal conditions and perhaps during pathological addictive-like states.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Nat.+Rev.+Neurosci.&amp;rft.atitle=Transcriptional+and+epigenetic+mechanisms+of+addiction&amp;rft.volume=12&amp;rft.issue=11&amp;rft.pages=623-37&amp;rft.date=2011-11&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3272277%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F21989194&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1038%2Fnrn3111&amp;rft.aulast=Robison&amp;rft.aufirst=AJ&amp;rft.au=Nestler%2C+EJ&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3272277&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAddiction"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Systematic_review_-_yet_another_DSM_fail-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Systematic_review_-_yet_another_DSM_fail_18-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Systematic_review_-_yet_another_DSM_fail_18-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Systematic_review_-_yet_another_DSM_fail_18-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Systematic_review_-_yet_another_DSM_fail_18-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFKarilaWéryWeinsteinCottencin2014">Karila L, Wéry A, Weinstein A, Cottencin O, Petit A, Reynaud M, Billieux J (2014). "Sexual addiction or hypersexual disorder: different terms for the same problem? A review of the literature". <i>Curr. Pharm. Des</i>. <b>20</b> (25): 4012–20. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.2174%2F13816128113199990619" rel="nofollow">10.2174/13816128113199990619</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/24001295" rel="nofollow">24001295</a>. <q>Sexual addiction, which is also known as hypersexual disorder, has largely been ignored by psychiatrists, even though the condition causes serious psychosocial problems for many people. A lack of empirical evidence on sexual addiction is the result of the disease's complete absence from versions of the Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders. ... Existing prevalence rates of sexual addiction-related disorders range from 3% to 6%. Sexual addiction/hypersexual disorder is used as an umbrella construct to encompass various types of problematic behaviors, including excessive masturbation, cybersex, pornography use, sexual behavior with consenting adults, telephone sex, strip club visitation, and other behaviors. The adverse consequences of sexual addiction are similar to the consequences of other addictive disorders. Addictive, somatic and psychiatric disorders coexist with sexual addiction. In recent years, research on sexual addiction has proliferated, and screening instruments have increasingly been developed to diagnose or quantify sexual addiction disorders. In our systematic review of the existing measures, 22 questionnaires were identified. As with other behavioral addictions, the appropriate treatment of sexual addiction should combine pharmacological and psychological approaches.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Curr.+Pharm.+Des.&amp;rft.atitle=Sexual+addiction+or+hypersexual+disorder%3A+different+terms+for+the+same+problem%3F+A+review+of+the+literature&amp;rft.volume=20&amp;rft.issue=25&amp;rft.pages=4012-20&amp;rft.date=2014&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.2174%2F13816128113199990619&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F24001295&amp;rft.aulast=Karila&amp;rft.aufirst=L&amp;rft.au=W%C3%A9ry%2C+A&amp;rft.au=Weinstein%2C+A&amp;rft.au=Cottencin%2C+O&amp;rft.au=Petit%2C+A&amp;rft.au=Reynaud%2C+M&amp;rft.au=Billieux%2C+J&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAddiction"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_D1_signaling-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_D1_signaling_19-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_D1_signaling_19-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_D1_signaling_19-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_D1_signaling_19-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_D1_signaling_19-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFPitchersVialouNestlerLaviolette2013">Pitchers KK, Vialou V, Nestler EJ, Laviolette SR, Lehman MN, Coolen LM (February 2013). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3865508" rel="nofollow">"Natural and drug rewards act on common neural plasticity mechanisms with ΔFosB as a key mediator"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Journal_of_Neuroscience" title="The Journal of Neuroscience">The Journal of Neuroscience</a></i>. <b>33</b> (8): 3434–42. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1523%2FJNEUROSCI.4881-12.2013" rel="nofollow">10.1523/JNEUROSCI.4881-12.2013</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3865508" rel="nofollow">3865508</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/23426671" rel="nofollow">23426671</a>. <q>Drugs of abuse induce neuroplasticity in the natural reward pathway, specifically the nucleus accumbens (NAc), thereby causing development and expression of addictive behavior. ... Together, these findings demonstrate that drugs of abuse and natural reward behaviors act on common molecular and cellular mechanisms of plasticity that control vulnerability to drug addiction, and that this increased vulnerability is mediated by ΔFosB and its downstream transcriptional targets. ... Sexual behavior is highly rewarding (Tenk et al., 2009), and sexual experience causes sensitized drug-related behaviors, including cross-sensitization to amphetamine (Amph)-induced locomotor activity (Bradley and Meisel, 2001; Pitchers et al., 2010a) and enhanced Amph reward (Pitchers et al., 2010a). Moreover, sexual experience induces neural plasticity in the NAc similar to that induced by psychostimulant exposure, including increased dendritic spine density (Meisel and Mullins, 2006; Pitchers et al., 2010a), altered glutamate receptor trafficking, and decreased synaptic strength in prefrontal cortex-responding NAc shell neurons (Pitchers et al., 2012). Finally, periods of abstinence from sexual experience were found to be critical for enhanced Amph reward, NAc spinogenesis (Pitchers et al., 2010a), and glutamate receptor trafficking (Pitchers et al., 2012). These findings suggest that natural and drug reward experiences share common mechanisms of neural plasticity</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Journal+of+Neuroscience&amp;rft.atitle=Natural+and+drug+rewards+act+on+common+neural+plasticity+mechanisms+with+%CE%94FosB+as+a+key+mediator&amp;rft.volume=33&amp;rft.issue=8&amp;rft.pages=3434-42&amp;rft.date=2013-02&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3865508%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F23426671&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1523%2FJNEUROSCI.4881-12.2013&amp;rft.aulast=Pitchers&amp;rft.aufirst=KK&amp;rft.au=Vialou%2C+V&amp;rft.au=Nestler%2C+EJ&amp;rft.au=Laviolette%2C+SR&amp;rft.au=Lehman%2C+MN&amp;rft.au=Coolen%2C+LM&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3865508&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAddiction"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_NMDA_signaling-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_NMDA_signaling_20-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_NMDA_signaling_20-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_NMDA_signaling_20-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_NMDA_signaling_20-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Amph-Sex_X-sensitization_through_NMDA_signaling_20-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFBeloateWeemsCaseyWebb2016">Beloate LN, Weems PW, Casey GR, Webb IC, Coolen LM (February 2016). <li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFMeuleGearhardt2014">Meule A, Gearhardt AN (September 2014). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4179181" rel="nofollow">"Food addiction in the light of DSM-5"</a>. <i>Nutrients</i>. <b>6</b> (9): 3653–71. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.3390%2Fnu6093653" rel="nofollow">10.3390/nu6093653</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4179181" rel="nofollow">4179181</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/25230209" rel="nofollow">25230209</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Nutrients&amp;rft.atitle=Food+addiction+in+the+light+of+DSM-5&amp;rft.volume=6&amp;rft.issue=9&amp;rft.pages=3653-71&amp;rft.date=2014-09&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4179181%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F25230209&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.3390%2Fnu6093653&amp;rft.aulast=Meule&amp;rft.aufirst=A&amp;rft.au=Gearhardt%2C+AN&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4179181&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAddiction"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Behavioral_addictions-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Behavioral_addictions_23-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Behavioral_addictions_23-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Behavioral_addictions_23-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFGrantPotenzaWeinsteinGorelick2010">Grant JE, Potenza MN, Weinstein A, Gorelick DA (September 2010). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3164585" rel="nofollow">"Introduction to behavioral addictions"</a>. <i>Am. J. Drug Alcohol Abuse</i>. <b>36</b> (5): 233–241. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.3109%2F00952990.2010.491884" rel="nofollow">10.3109/00952990.2010.491884</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3164585" rel="nofollow">3164585</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/20560821" rel="nofollow">20560821</a>. <q>Naltrexone, a mu-opioid receptor antagonist approved by the US Food and Drug Administration for the treatment of alcoholism and opioid dependence, has shown efficacy in controlled clinical trials for the treatment of pathological gambling and kleptomania (76–79), and promise in uncontrolled studies of compulsive buying (80), compulsive sexual behavior (81), internet addiction (82), and pathologic skin picking (83). ... Topiramate, an anti-convulsant which blocks the AMPA subtype of glutamate receptor (among other actions), has shown promise in open-label studies of pathological gambling, compulsive buying, and compulsive skin picking (85), as well as efficacy in reducing alcohol (86), cigarette (87), and cocaine (88) use. N-acetyl cysteine, an amino acid that restores extracellular glutamate concentration in the nucleus accumbens, reduced gambling urges and behavior in one study of pathological gamblers (89), and reduces cocaine craving (90) and cocaine use (91) in cocaine addicts. These studies suggest that glutamatergic modulation of dopaminergic tone in the nucleus accumbens may be a mechanism common to behavioral addiction and substance use disorders (92).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Am.+J.+Drug+Alcohol+Abuse&amp;rft.atitle=Introduction+to+behavioral+addictions&amp;rft.volume=36&amp;rft.issue=5&amp;rft.pages=233-241&amp;rft.date=2010-09&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3164585%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F20560821&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.3109%2F00952990.2010.491884&amp;rft.aulast=Grant&amp;rft.aufirst=JE&amp;rft.au=Potenza%2C+MN&amp;rft.au=Weinstein%2C+A&amp;rft.au=Gorelick%2C+DA&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3164585&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAddiction"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFFehrmanEganGorbanLevesley2019">Fehrman E, Egan V, Gorban AN, Levesley J, Mirkes EM, Muhammad AK (2019). <i>Personality Traits and Drug Consumption. A Story Told by Data</i>. <li id="cite_note-Dawe-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Dawe_29-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFDaweLoxton2004">Dawe S, Loxton NJ (May 2004). "The role of impulsivity in the development of substance use and eating disorders". <i>Neurosci Biobehav Rev</i>. <b>28</b> (3): 343–51. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.neubiorev.2004.03.007" rel="nofollow">10.1016/j.neubiorev.2004.03.007</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/15225976" rel="nofollow">15225976</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a> <a class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:24435589" rel="nofollow">24435589</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Neurosci+Biobehav+Rev&amp;rft.atitle=The+role+of+impulsivity+in+the+development+of+substance+use+and+eating+disorders&amp;rft.volume=28&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=343-51&amp;rft.date=2004-05&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A24435589%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F15225976&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.neubiorev.2004.03.007&amp;rft.aulast=Dawe&amp;rft.aufirst=S&amp;rft.au=Loxton%2C+NJ&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAddiction"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Cognitive_-_stimulus-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Cognitive_-_stimulus_30-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFWashburn2016">Washburn DA (2016). "The Stroop effect at 80: The competition between stimulus control and cognitive control". <i>J Exp Anal Behav</i>. <b>105</b> (1): 3–13. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1002%2Fjeab.194" rel="nofollow">10.1002/jeab.194</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26781048" rel="nofollow">26781048</a>. <q>Today, arguably more than at any time in history, the constructs of attention, executive functioning, and cognitive control seem to be pervasive and preeminent in research and theory. Even within the cognitive framework, however, there has long been an understanding that behavior is multiply determined, and that many responses are relatively automatic, unattended, contention-scheduled, and habitual. Indeed, the cognitive flexibility, response inhibition, and self-regulation that appear to be hallmarks of cognitive control are noteworthy only in contrast to responses that are relatively rigid, associative, and involuntary.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=J+Exp+Anal+Behav&amp;rft.atitle=The+Stroop+effect+at+80%3A+The+competition+between+stimulus+control+and+cognitive+control&amp;rft.volume=105&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.pages=3-13&amp;rft.date=2016&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1002%2Fjeab.194&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F26781048&amp;rft.aulast=Washburn&amp;rft.aufirst=DA&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAddiction"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Executive_functions-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Executive_functions_31-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFDiamond2013">Diamond A (2013). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4084861" rel="nofollow">"Executive functions"</a>. <i>Annu Rev Psychol</i>. <b>64</b>: 135–68. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1146%2Fannurev-psych-113011-143750" rel="nofollow">10.1146/annurev-psych-113011-143750</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4084861" rel="nofollow">4084861</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/23020641" rel="nofollow">23020641</a>. <q>Core EFs are inhibition [response inhibition (self-control – resisting temptations and resisting acting impulsively) and interference control (selective attention and cognitive inhibition)], working memory, and cognitive flexibility (including creatively thinking "outside the box," seeing anything from different perspectives, and quickly and flexibly adapting to changed circumstances). ... EFs and prefrontal cortex are the first to suffer, and suffer disproportionately, if something is not right in your life. They suffer first, and most, if you are stressed (Arnsten 1998, Liston et al. 2009, Oaten &amp; Cheng 2005), sad (Hirt et al. 2008, von Hecker &amp; Meiser 2005), lonely (Baumeister et al. 2002, Cacioppo &amp; Patrick 2008, Campbell et al. 2006, Tun et al. 2012), sleep deprived (Barnes et al. 2012, Huang et al. 2007), or not physically fit (Best 2010, Chaddock et al. 2011, Hillman et al. 2008). Any of these can cause you to appear to have a disorder of EFs, such as ADHD, when you do not. You can see the deleterious effects of stress, sadness, loneliness, and lack of physical health or fitness at the physiological and neuroanatomical level in prefrontal cortex and at the behavioral level in worse EFs (poorer reasoning and problem solving, forgetting things, and impaired ability to exercise discipline and self-control). ...<br/>EFs can be improved (Diamond &amp; Lee 2011, Klingberg 2010). ... At any age across the life cycle EFs can be improved, including in the elderly and in infants. There has been much work with excellent results on improving EFs in the elderly by improving physical fitness (Erickson &amp; Kramer 2009, Voss et al. 2011) ... Inhibitory control (one of the core EFs) involves being able to control one's attention, behavior, thoughts, and/or emotions to override a strong internal predisposition or external lure, and instead do what's more appropriate or needed. Without inhibitory control we would be at the mercy of impulses, old habits of thought or action (conditioned responses), and/or stimuli in the environment that pull us this way or that. Thus, inhibitory control makes it possible for us to change and for us to choose how we react and how we behave rather than being unthinking creatures of habit. It doesn't make it easy. Indeed, we usually are creatures of habit and our behavior is under the control of environmental stimuli far more than we usually realize, but having the ability to exercise inhibitory control creates the possibility of change and choice. ... The subthalamic nucleus appears to play a critical role in preventing such impulsive or premature responding (Frank 2006).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Annu+Rev+Psychol&amp;rft.atitle=Executive+functions&amp;rft.volume=64&amp;rft.pages=135-68&amp;rft.date=2013&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4084861%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F23020641&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1146%2Fannurev-psych-113011-143750&amp;rft.aulast=Diamond&amp;rft.aufirst=A&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4084861&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAddiction"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-NHM-Cognitive_Control-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-NHM-Cognitive_Control_32-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-NHM-Cognitive_Control_32-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFMalenkaNestlerHyman2009">Malenka RC, Nestler EJ, Hyman SE (2009). "Chapter 13: Higher Cognitive Function and Behavioral Control".  In Sydor A, Brown RY (eds.). <i>Molecular Neuropharmacology: A Foundation for Clinical Neuroscience</i> (2nd ed.). New York: McGraw-Hill Medical. pp. 313–21. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-07-148127-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-07-148127-4"><bdi>978-0-07-148127-4</bdi></a>. <q> •  Executive function, the cognitive control of behavior, depends on the prefrontal cortex, which is highly developed in higher primates and especially humans.<br/> •  Working memory is a short-term, capacity-limited cognitive buffer that stores information and permits its manipulation to guide decision-making and behavior. ...<br/>These diverse inputs and back projections to both cortical and subcortical structures put the prefrontal cortex in a position to exert what is often called "top-down" control or cognitive control of behavior. ... The prefrontal cortex receives inputs not only from other cortical regions, including association cortex, but also, via the thalamus, inputs from subcortical structures subserving emotion and motivation, such as the amygdala (Chapter 14) and ventral striatum (or nucleus accumbens; Chapter 15). ...<br/>In conditions in which prepotent responses tend to dominate behavior, such as in drug addiction, where drug cues can elicit drug seeking (Chapter 15), or in attention deficit hyperactivity disorder (ADHD; described below), significant negative consequences can result. ... ADHD can be conceptualized as a disorder of executive function; specifically, ADHD is characterized by reduced ability to exert and maintain cognitive control of behavior. Compared with healthy individuals, those with ADHD have diminished ability to suppress inappropriate prepotent responses to stimuli (impaired response inhibition) and diminished ability to inhibit responses to irrelevant stimuli (impaired interference suppression). ... Functional neuroimaging in humans demonstrates activation of the prefrontal cortex and caudate nucleus (part of the striatum) in tasks that demand inhibitory control of behavior. Subjects with ADHD exhibit less activation of the medial prefrontal cortex than healthy controls even when they succeed in such tasks and utilize different circuits. ... Early results with structural MRI show thinning of the cerebral cortex in ADHD subjects compared with age-matched controls in prefrontal cortex and posterior parietal cortex, areas involved in working memory and attention.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Chapter+13%3A+Higher+Cognitive+Function+and+Behavioral+Control&amp;rft.btitle=Molecular+Neuropharmacology%3A+A+Foundation+for+Clinical+Neuroscience&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pages=313-21&amp;rft.edition=2nd&amp;rft.pub=McGraw-Hill+Medical&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-07-148127-4&amp;rft.aulast=Malenka&amp;rft.aufirst=RC&amp;rft.au=Nestler%2C+EJ&amp;rft.au=Hyman%2C+SE&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAddiction"></span></span>
</li>

rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFVassolerSadri-Vakili2014">Vassoler FM, Sadri-Vakili G (2014). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3872494" rel="nofollow">"Mechanisms of transgenerational inheritance of addictive-like behaviors"</a>. <i>Neuroscience</i>. <b>264</b>: 198–206. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.neuroscience.2013.07.064" rel="nofollow">10.1016/j.neuroscience.2013.07.064</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3872494" rel="nofollow">3872494</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/23920159" rel="nofollow">23920159</a>. <q>However, the components that are responsible for the heritability of characteristics that make an individual more susceptible to drug addiction in humans remain largely unknown given that patterns of inheritance cannot be explained by simple genetic mechanisms (Cloninger et al., 1981; Schuckit et al., 1972). The environment also plays a large role in the development of addiction as evidenced by great societal variability in drug use patterns between countries and across time (UNODC, 2012). Therefore, both genetics and the environment contribute to an individual's vulnerability to become addicted following an initial exposure to drugs of abuse. ...<br/>The evidence presented here demonstrates that rapid environmental adaptation occurs following exposure to a number of stimuli. Epigenetic mechanisms represent the key components by which the environment can influence genetics, and they provide the missing link between genetic heritability and environmental influences on the behavioral and physiological phenotypes of the offspring.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Neuroscience&amp;rft.atitle=Mechanisms+of+transgenerational+inheritance+of+addictive-like+behaviors&amp;rft.volume=264&amp;rft.pages=198-206&amp;rft.date=2014&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3872494%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F23920159&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.neuroscience.2013.07.064&amp;rft.aulast=Vassoler&amp;rft.aufirst=FM&amp;rft.au=Sadri-Vakili%2C+G&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3872494&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAddiction"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-pmid26572641-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-pmid26572641_59-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-pmid26572641_59-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-pmid26572641_59-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFYuanLiSunOuyang2015">Yuan TF, Li A, Sun X, Ouyang H, Campos C, Rocha NB, Arias-Carrión O, Machado S, Hou G, So KF (2015). Neurobiol</i>. <b>53</b> (9): 6367–76. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1007%2Fs12035-015-9526-2" rel="nofollow">10.1007/s12035-015-9526-2</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hdl_(identifier)" title="Hdl (identifier)">hdl</a>:<span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//hdl.handle.net/10400.22%2F7331" rel="nofollow">10400.22/7331</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26572641" rel="nofollow">26572641</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a> <a class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:25694221" rel="nofollow">25694221</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Mol.+Neurobiol.&amp;rft.atitle=Transgenerational+Inheritance+of+Paternal+Neurobehavioral+Phenotypes%3A+Stress%2C+Addiction%2C+Ageing+and+Metabolism&amp;rft.volume=53&amp;rft.issue=9&amp;rft.pages=6367-76&amp;rft.date=2015&amp;rft_id=info%3Ahdl%2F10400.22%2F7331&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A25694221%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F26572641&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1007%2Fs12035-015-9526-2&amp;rft.aulast=Yuan&amp;rft.aufirst=TF&amp;rft.au=Li%2C+A&amp;rft.au=Sun%2C+X&amp;rft.au=Ouyang%2C+H&amp;rft.au=Campos%2C+C&amp;rft.au=Rocha%2C+NB&amp;rft.au=Arias-Carri%C3%B3n%2C+O&amp;rft.au=Machado%2C+S&amp;rft.au=Hou%2C+G&amp;rft.au=So%2C+KF&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAddiction"></span></span>
